tentlon- The soldiers were stationed

mounted gendarmes a hundred yards
off.

dictment. i
M. Chamoine, the custodian of the
famous secret dossier, said it would take
four days to examine it, as the examina
tion would be behind, closed doors and
most of the witnesses have gone to the
seaside General de Boisdeffre to Dinard
and General Dionne. to Param. General
Mercier remains here," the guest of Gen
eral Saint Germain, who tells his friends
to be prepared for sensational news that
must crush Dreyfus and many others with
him.
M. Cavaignac talked at Le Mars on his
way here. He said he expected two coups j
de theatre. We now hear that one will
be an avowal by General Billot that he j
said on his soul and conscience Dreyfus j
was a traitor because he was misled. The
other will be Mercier driven to bay. The i
latter will issUe from this trial ruined if !
Dreyfus is acquitted. I. hear he intends j
to throw a searchlight on the secret coun- ,
cils of the Dupuy government, of which
he was a member in 1894, and to show
what diplomatic wires were pulled be
tween the arrest of Dreyfus -and his em
barkation for Cayenne. Mercier refuses
to be interviewed. He looks bowed
down from care.
General Billot, on the other hand, seems
to have taken out a new lease of life. He
is also extremely reticent as, indeed, are
all the witnesses of note.
M. Jaures arrived last night, j He claims
to know much that is in the secret dos
sier. An Intercepted correspondence be
tween two military attaches who were
mixed up in the affair is., said by Jaures
to be a shame to human, nature. . These
two officers wrote under feminine names
and in a strain that might not have
shocked the young courtiers of Nero or
the ribald crowd that gathered around
Lot's house in the doomed city. Possibly,
nay, probably, the attaches in question
never wrote these scented anacreontic bil
lets, for such they are. Le Mercier Pick
ard was equal to the task of forging them
and he possibly did forge them for a pc
! cuniary ' consideration. Hence, perhaps,
! the mjstery in which his suicide is
! shrouded.

HURRICANE
Trying' Experience of the
Bessie Stevens En

WATER SUPPLY LOST
The Vessel Was So Badly Dam
aged That She Was Obliged to
Put Into Kobe for Repairs.
VICTORIA. B. C. Aug. B.â Copies
of the Kobe Chronicle received
by the Empress of Japan con
tain interviews with Captain
Benjamin Havner and the ship's
company of the schooner Bessie Stev
ens, the vessel recently built at San
.Francisco. They had a most exciting
story to relate of adventures In a storm
encounter* July -4. The Bessie Stevens,
according to the story told by them,
left San Francisco* for Guam with .*-.
party of investigators and provisions
for the traders on the islands.
Guam was reached on June 24 and
instructions were received there to pro
ceed to Hongkong and communicate
with Manila by cable. On July 4. about
the middle of the northeast trades in
longitude 134 degree* 52 minutes east
and latitude 16 degrees 54 minutes
north, the Stevens encountered a severe
hurricane from the southeast, which
lasted for four days. The glass on the
nth fell to 29.40, which was the lowest
' reading recorded.
Captain Havner, who has been four
' teen years at ( sea, says that he has
never experienced a hurricane of such
; force. â¢
The waves ran. he pays, over fifty feet j
,'high. and what was worse, broke. On j
the third day of the gale an enormous
wave struck the schooner amidships
and, the ballast shifting, the vessel was ,
thrown on her beam ends. As good luck 1
would have it. no other wave struck
her while in this position, and before
the next big wave came the sailors |
managed to get some of the ballast
shifted and the boat on an even keel, j
The feelings of Captain Havner may be
better imagined than described when it j
is stated that he had his wife and child I
on board. Of course, a small boat was!
; prepared for use in th** event of the I
I schooner foundering, i3l*_ -'â¢.â â ., i
tain said: "One does that sort of thing ;
out of habit, but it would save time to
go down with the vessel. A small boat
in such a sea could not have lived five
| minutes." â  . . :....,*.
The schooner, as might be expected,
suffered severe damage. She was bad
ly strained and leaking, and after the
storm part of the cabins had to be torn
away to find the leak. The head sail
was also blown away, and the main
topmast and the galley and booby
hatch washed overboard.
To add to the misfortunes of the voy
age, the shifting of the ballast when
the schooner was thrown on her beam
ends caused a water cask to ' urst, and
for some time the crew had to be put
on short allowance.
Captain Havner found it best with
the prevailing winds to put into Kobe
for repairs, * hich will probably take a
considerable time.
Telegraphic Communication Is Cut
Off East of Cuba.
HAVANA, Aug. B.â The Weather Bu
reau here has warned all stations in Cuba
as well as Kingston, Jamaica, that tele
graphic communication is cut off east of
Cuba. The hurricane center is apparently
approaching Porto Rico and will probably
pass north of that island.

SCHOONER BESSIE STEVENS.
NEW CAMPAIGN IN THE
PHILIPPINES COMMENCED
Mac Arthur, With Four Thousand
Men, Defeats Six Thousand
Rebels at San Fernando.
-rifl-ANILA, Aug. 9, 12:25 p. m.â General Mac Arthur's force, consisting of 4000 men, advanced
MANILA, Aug. 12:25 p. Fernando to-day and encountered consisting 4000 men. force of
five miles beyond San Fernando to-day and encountered and defeated a Filipino force of
I 6000 men. , , .......
nÂ± - The enemy retreated, leaving many dead and wounded. The American loss in killed and
wounded was twenty.
There has been a strong suspicion that for some time past insurgents were among sup
posedly peaceful natives entering Manila.
The Provost Marshal took steps to learn if these suspicions were true, and soon discovered
that rebels had entered the city and were actively plotting to start riots. They were also en
gaged in smuggling arms for the enemy.
At midnight last night men engaged in hunting down the conspirators located their head
quarters in a cigar factory in Binondo. * A company of the Sixth Infantry, under command of Cap
tain Ames, was at once dispatched to the. place, which was surrounded. The soldiers raided the
factory and captured forty-three rebels, including a number of officers belonging to the Filipino
army. There were more men in the building, but they managed to escape by means of the roof
of the factory and adjoining buildings. It is believed the authorities have succeeded in capturing
a powerful gang that was giving valuable service to the enemy.

MANILA.. Aug. Several
days ago General Mac
Arthur's spies learned
from unquestioned sources of a
plot for a native uprising which
included the massacre of General
Mac Arthur and the whole garri
son of the city of San Fernando,
which the United States troops
have occupied since its capture
several months ago.
Soon after the discovery of the
plot the Macabebes, the ancient
enemies of the Tagalos. sent dep
utations to General Mat-Arthur
professing ardent friendship for
the United States and begging
that they be given arms with
which to fight the rebels. The

OTIS.
general, knowing of the conspir
acy, instead of meeting the
wishes of the deputation, gave
orders for their immediate ban
ishment from the city. Notwith
standing their protestations of

pathizers, for such they were,

Aguinaldo has appealed to the powers
for recognition of "Filipino independ
ence" in'a document dated from Tarlac,
July 27, and signed by Buencaminho.
It has beer received by all the foreign
Consuls in Manila, with the request
that they forward it to their respective
Governments.
The Filipinos use their old argument
that they had conquered the sover
, eignty of these islands from Spain
before the signing of the treaty of
Paris and therefore Spain was in no
; position to. cede them to the United
States. They argue that the possession
; of the 7000 Spanish prisoners, captured
â  with their arms, fighting against the
Filipinos, is eloquent proof of the nullity
of the Spanish sovereignty, as when
they surrendered Spain's hold was irre
vocably lost. The document then says:
Replying to the Spanish Commission's
request to release the prisoners because
Spain no longer has political interests in
the islands, we asked for a treaty of
. peace and friendship between Spain and
the Filipinos. whereby the prisoners
i would be released. But the commission
ers refused, because it would mean r< c
ofnition of our Independence. This is
i equivalent to saying that the prisoners
I must stay in our hands indefinitely lie
. cause their possession is our most effica-
I cious method to adjust our account with
Spain and obtain from her recognition of
1 independence.
The Filipinos claim that they con
quered all the country except Manila,
and that they co-operated, in securing
the latter'-* capitulation by surround
ing it at the cost of thousands of lives.
They also claim they conquered the
country unassisted, except for sixty
guns that Admiral Dewey gave Agui
naldo, and that Admiral - Dewey and
the British and Belgian Consuls rec
ognized the Filipino sovereignty by
asking for passes to visit the country.
They claim that they have ' letters
from American Consuls and generals
recognizing their sovereignty and
promising that the Americans would
recognize their independence, "which
was at the disposition of the powers."
The Filipinos attempt to make capital
I of the statement that Admiral Dewey
i had such confidence Aguinaldo would
j observe and fulfill the rules of war
that he gave him a hundred Spanish
prisoners which the American navy
had captured.
Finally; the Filipinos appeal to the
powers to influence Washington to
bring a termination to the unjust war
which is devastating the country.

Department is entirely without in
formation as to the address by Aguin
aldo to foreign powers tor the recogni
tion of the Filipinos, either as belliger
ents or as constituting an Independent
government. The appeal Is looked
upon, however, as creating no particu
lar change, as it has been the desire
of Aguinaldo and associates from the
lirst to secure such recognition. The
appeal puts this desire Into rather
formal shape, and it may bring to an
Issue the position of outside govern
ments. There is no idea here that any
foreign government- will heed the ad
dress. The international usage as to
recognition was q*ilte fully developed
during the Cuban insurrection, at
which time the question was exhaust
lively discussed in Congress and was
the subject of lengthy consideration in
the messages of the President. The
general view at that time was that
recognition of belligerency was not In
itself a casus belli. Up to the present
time there has been no intimation to
the Stat. Department that any foreign
power entertained even the remotest
Idea of giving recognition to Aguinaldo
and his associates. On the contrary,
there has been most friendly feeling
maintained between this Government
and foreign powers throughout the
Filipino conflict. 1 .
Some time at-., General Otis made
requisition on the War Department for
2500 horses to mount the cavalry in the
Philippines. He stated in the dispatch
that the horses in the islands were not
satisfactory and. that the contracts for
them in China had failed, tin- words
'Contraband of war being added.
The report from General Otis thai
China, had refused to permit the ship
ment to Manila of horses purchased at
Tientsin by his agents was made the
subject of an official representation to
the Peking Government, and late re
ports received from Minister Conger
show that the prohibition was not par
ticularly applied! against the United
States, but against Germany as well,
and that the exportation of mules and
horses is forbidden by law. Neverthe
less Minister Conger satisfied himself
that there is no cause for alarm at the
action of the Chinese Government and
officials are confident from his report
that China will not recognize the Inde
pendence of the Insurgents. Concern
ing the results of his investigation this
statement was made at the State De
partment to-day: "On making Inquiry
at the Department of Stat.- concerning
the report that the Chinese Government
had refused permission "or the expor
tation of horses and mules from China
to the Philippine Islands on the ground
thai they were contraband of war, it is
ascertained that early in June Minister
Conger reported to the Department of
State that the United. states quarter
-1 master at Manila, having purchased
seventy-five ponies and twenty-five
i mules at Tientsin, an export pass was
refused and that upon . application to
the Chinese Tsung-Ll-Yamen, or Coun
cil of State, for permission to export
the animals, Mr. Conger was informed
that such permission would be granted
to the United States Government if it
were possible to grant it to any gov
ernment, but that for a long time the
export of ponies and mules had been
forbidden by law and on several recent
occasions had been refused to other
governments, so that no exception
could be well made .in favor of the
United States.
"Mr. Conger explains that native po
nies are needed for use in the Chinese
army, for which reason their exporta
tion is prohibited, except In small lots
of ponies claimed to be for racing;, polo,
etc. So far as Mr. Conger can ascertain
no large lots have been permitted to be
sent to any of the foreign stations in
China or to any foreign governments
since 1888.
"In a later dispatch Mr. Conger re
ports that while the Taotai at Tientsin
bad permitted the shipment of thirty
mules to Manila a second request for a
shipment of 150 had been refused. Mr.
Conger added that a similar refusal
had been made upon the application of
the German legation to send sixty po
nies to Kiaochau."
The importance of success in the
coming fall campaign is thoroughly ap
preciated by administration officials,
who understand that If the insurgents
should at its close lie as strong as they
are "to-day, the difficulty of preventing
recognition of their belligerency will be
materially increased.
The course which the foreign Consuls
at Manila will pursue in connection
with Aguinaldo's communication Is a
matter of some conjecture among offi
cials here. At the State Department
there is no disposition to limit the Con
suls in forwarding the appeal l 0 their
respective governments. As a rule it is
quite unusual for Consuls holding exe
quaturs from a friendly government
and having official relation with it to
carry on communication with an Insur
rectionary leader, but it is appreciated
that in this case the Consuls are the
innocent victims of Aguinaldo's letter
writing.
As military authority is supreme in
the Philippines any question arising as
to the course of the Consuls would
probably be referred to General Otis,
but there is no Intimation here that he
has been advised of the address or that
he will have any objections to its trans
mission by the Consuls. It is rather ex
pected, however, that the Consuls will
take occasion to advise with the mili
tary authorities as to the proprieties
â of the situation before taking final
action.
That the officers and men of the
Asiatic squadron may be in condition
to heartily assist the army in its fall
operations against Aguinaldo's forces
Acting Secretary Allen has decided to
instruct Rear Admiral Watson to dis
patch his vessels in rotation to Yoko
hama. Japan. The vessels will remain
a month each in Japanese waters, and
any of the men who may be debilitated
will be left in the naval hospital at Yo
kohama until they are entirely recov
ered and lit to resume duty. The deci
sion of Mr. Allen In this matter was the
result of a conference he had with Sur
geon General Van Reypen. The deci
sion of Mr. Allen is not due, I was told.
to any fear of disease among the crews
of vessels, but to a desire to keep the
men in good condition.

Special Dispatch to The 'all.
STOCKTON, Aug. 8.- A party of sur-
veyors under Civil Engineer H. T.
Compton will in a lew days commence
running preliminary lines between here
and Sacramento for a railroad. It is
believed the work is preliminary to a
j complete survey for persons who have
| an understanding with the Santa Fe
i Company, A number of local capital
j ists are interested in the project. As
i soon as enough data can be obtained
| a detailed report will be forwarded to
| the railroad men, and if satisfactory
it is expected encouragement will be
i given to local capitalists to begin the
work of ruction.
Compton acknowledges that the lines
are for a railroad, but declines to dis
cuss the matter further. It is consid
ered probable thai one object of the
survey Is to ascertain what encourage
ment could be obtained from residents
along the route in the matter of rights
of way.
It is proposed to make the line as
nearly straight as possible. The first
survey will begin west of the lower
Sacramento road and continue due
north to New Hope. It is believed here
that the people of the valley will wel
come an extension of the Santa Ft and
give all reasonable encouragement to
the company's plans.

THE WAR AS SEEN BY
EPWORTH LEAGUERS
DES MOINES, Aug. B.â joint debate
between Governor M. L. Shaw and Gen
eral J. B. Weaver on the Philippine ques
tion was the feature of the Epworth
League assembly, at Colfax to-day. The
debate attracted an audience of several
thousand persons from the surrounding
country. Both speakers are finished ora
tors and presented the question in the
best possible light from opposite stand- |
points. . , , __
General Weaver attacked, in the main,
the policy of the Government In the Phil
ippines, claming it was In conflict with
the constitution, the Declaration of In
dependence, the Monro.- doctrine, the
laws of nations and the holy precepts of
our religion.
Governor Shaw upheld the Republican
administration in every respect, declaring
that he was for "a broader policy and
advancement of the higher and newer
Civilisation which confronts the American
people to-day." _

NEGRO WRESTED FROM
POLICE AND LYNCHED
ALEXANDRIA, A' a.. Aug. B.â Ben
Thompson, colored, 20 years of age,
was lynched here at 11:30 o'clock to
night for having attempted an assault
on Lillian Clarke, the 8-year-old daugh
ter of Edward Clarke, residing on Pa
trick street. The police made a de
termined effort to prevent the mob,
which gathered around the jail at about
11 o'clock, obtaining possession of the
prisoner, but ' were unsuccessful. . .
The door was broken In and during
the scuffle which ensued Chief of Police
Webster was hurt." The prisoner was
: found in the cellar of the building and
brought out, but the report became
current that he was not the man, and
he was released. He had gone but a
short distance, however, when the mob
concluded that Thompson was the man
they were looking for and after a short
chase be was captured and strung up
to an electric light pole. The. prisoner
was roughly handled on the way to the
place of hanging, his clothes being cut
almost to shreds.
During the scenes at the front of the
Jail Mayor Sampson addressed the
crowd, imploring it to leave the pris
oner alone and promising to call the
Grand Jury for early action on the
case, but his pleadings were without
i avail.

Governor Atkinson was one of the leaders of the Democratic party hi
th ._ state He was elected Governor in 1896, at the age of 39. after having
served sift years in the House of Representatives, being Speaker for several

een months. Â»______

0 an era of electric railway building.

WICKERSHAM WILL
CONTEST STOPPED
A Settlement Made Out
of Court.
FRANK WILL GET A SHARE
HE HAD BEEN CUT OFF WITH
FIVE DOLLARS.
In the Suit the Claim Was Made That
the Father Had Been Unduly
Influenced by His Younger
Son, Fred. .
Special Dispatch to The Call.
. â . â¢
SANTA ROSA, Aug. B.â The cele
brated Wickersham will contest, of
which so much was expected in the
way of sensational details when the
matter came to trial, has been settled
amicably out of court. By the terms
of the agreement entered Into to-day
Frank Wickersham; the . well-known
Fresno capitalist, is to receive an equit
able share in the estate. left by his
father, the late I. G. Wickersham of
Petaluma, and the contest proceedings
are to be withdrawn at once.
I. G. Wickersham, the well-known
Petaluma banker who died some six
weeks ago, left an estate valued at con
siderably over a million dollars. Sur
viving him were four children, Frank
Wickersham of Fresno, Mrs. Bergeven
of Chicago and Fred a Wickersham and
Miss Lizzie Wickersham oosf s Petaluma.
Each was left an equal portion of the
estate by the terms of the will with the
exception of Frank,' who was cut off
with the proverbial penny, amounting
in this case to $5 in gold.
As a reason for his action in thus dis
inheriting his eldest son the old banker
explained that he had already advanced
him considerable over $100,000 in cash,
and this was all to which he was en
titled. The day after the will was
filed for probate the disinherited son
began contest proceedings to have the
will set aside upon the ground that his
father had been unduly influenced by
his brother Fred Wickersham of Peta
luma. The case attracted widespread
interest both on account of the alle
gations made and the prominence of
the parties concerned.
News that the matter had been sat
isfactorily adjusted reached here late
this evening and Fred A. Wickersham
was at once seen in regard to the mat
ter. ' He substantiated the report and
stated that he and his brother had met
to-day in the office of R. Porter Ashe,
the San Francisco attorney, where all
details of the settlement had been ad
justed to the mutual satisfaction of all
concerned.
In discussing the matter he said that
as soon as the contest proceedings were
begun negotiations looking to a settle
ment had been opened. A number of
propositions had been made back and
forth, none of which proved satisfac
tory. At the meeting held this after
noon Fred A. Wickersham, upon behalf
of himself and the other heirs, made
his final offer, which being deemed
equitable was at once agreed to. A
payment upon account was at once
made anjl a receipt taken therefor, and
as soon as the necessary papers cover
ing the agreement can be drawn up the
contest proceedings now pending in the
courts of this â¢ county will be with
drawn.
Mr. Wickersham declined to state
the terms of the agreement entered in
to, but it is understood that the con
testant receives approximately his full
, share of the estate less the amount he
; has already received from time to time
I and which he has never denied having
I been paid by his deceased father. With
I the signing of the, receipt mentioned in
j Porter Ashe's office to-day the entire
I case was brought to a close and har
| mony once more restored to the "Wick-
I ersham household.

CONDEMNS THE LYNCHING.
I Romano's Report on the Killing of
Italians in Louisiana.
"WASHINGTON, Aug. The State De
partment has received from the Italian
authorities a copy of a complete report
made by the Marquis Romano, secretary
of the Italian embassy here, on his per
sonal investigation of the recent lynching
of five Italians at Tallulah. La. The re
port presents the affair in a serious light.
As a whole the Italian authorities view
the* matter of the lynching as one of un
usual atrocity, and without the mitigating
circumstances which had been urged tn
previous affairs of this kind.

DIED IN AWFUL AGONY
Wreck of the- Los Angeles Express at Dos
Palos Cost the Lives of Two Men,
.ENGINES* JOf]N C FOT*D â
FRESNO, Aug. S.â Picture in your
mind's eye a long string of cars
snapped, as It were, like a black
snake cracked by some mighty
power and lying curled along the
railroad reservation, on either side of
which are stretches of wheat fields just
being tinted by the sun rising over the
mountains: imagine everything twisted
and splintered and wrought out of shape;
Bee a crowd of curious countryfolkâ men
in harvest dress and women and chil
dren in calicoâ and with them glum and
scarred railroad men, and you have a
rough sketch of the scene of the wreck
of the Los Angeles express, which, as "as
reported in this morning's Call, occurred
a half mile this side of Do's Pah).- last
night. Such was the scene when at 6
o'clock this morning Coroner Long and
The Call correspondent arrived on a spe
cial engine at the place of the disaster.
A picture sadder by far was that seen
in the baggage-room at Dos Palos. There
lay two mute and mutilated men â En
gineer Ford and Fireman Woodsâ who
had lost their lives. The bodies jay on
trucks, covered by large blankets. The
men had been scalded so badly that In
many places the flesh had dropped, ex
posing the bones. Friends had bandaged
the burns while the men were still alive
and the bandages were allowed to re
main and held the flesh in place. The
clothes of the men were drenched, not
with water, but with the scalding steam
that burned them.
After Coroner Long had summoned a
Jury the first thing was to view the re
mains. In searching the body of the en
gineer the Coroner took from the vsst
pocket his watch, which v. as running and
â¢â hewed the correct time.
Immediately after the wreck a special
train was made up at Tracy and sent to
the scene of the disaster. It had arrived
before the Coroner, and hence the state
ments of the passengers could not be ob
tained there by him. for the passenge-s
were taken back to Tracy and thence sent
over the main route down the valley. A.
rumber of the Fresno passengers reached
here at 1:30 o'clock this afternoon. This
special train hud attached to it three of
the Pullman coaches of the wrecked train
â the last three, which had not been de
railed.
The first Intimation of anything being
wrong came to the railroad men shortly
after leaving Dos Palos. when the train
began to get "wobbly.'' Conductor Harry
W Bridges says he was in the first sleep
er and he felt the train jarring. He im
mediately concluded that It was off the
track and rung the whistle to warn the
engineer. The latter applied the airbrake
end made what is called an emergency
<=top. The train was going at the rate of
thirty-five miles an hour. Before the con
ductor could pull the string again the air
brakes had been applied and in an Instant
the cars had been hurled off the track
in all directions. Only the last four Pull
mans held the rails.
The engine was hurled In the ditch.
DOUBLE TRAGEDY
AT NEVADA CITY
Charles Moreno Kills a
Woman.Then Himself.
Sp**-**!*"" Dispatch to Th* Call.
NEVADA CITY, Aug. B.â Charles
Moreno, a half-breed Spaniard aged 25
years, and a young and attractive
woman of the half-world known both as
Clara Wallace and Mrs. Lizzie Riley
are to-night lying dead at the Rocky
Bar House, a place of bad repute in
the outskirts of the little mining town
of Washington, in this county.
Shortly after 9 o'clock this morning
Moreno killed her and then took his
own life. He had long been infatuated
with the woman, and according to a note
that he left for the Coroner had a year
ago left his own wife to bask in her
smiles. Last spring the woman had de
serted him at 18 Turk street, San Fran
cisco, where they were living together,
and had come to this county. He could
not live without her, he declared, and
as she refused to return â¢ to him he
thought it better that both of them
wind up their troublous career here to
be happily reunited in another world.
Moreno arrived in this city on last
evening's train, and armed with a re
volver and a murderous dirk started
on foot for Washington, nineteen miles
above here. Upon his arrival there he
went to the house where the object of
his mad infatuation lived With Mollie
Walsh, keeping a den of the vilest de
scription.
His unexpected appearance crease
consternation on the part of the woman
he sought. He demanded that she at
once leave with him. She refused and
a violent quarrel ensued. She stood her
ground and ordered him to leave. With
out warning he fired two shots. Both
entered her breast, one piercing her
heart. Her body plunged headlong
from the door in which she stood to
the ground.
Then Moreno wrote the note that the
Coroner found, and having signed it
stood near her corpse and fired at his
own breast, the bullet striking a bone
and making only a slight wound. Next
he put the muzzle of the weapon to his
head, and sending a ball Into his brain
sank lifeless by his victim's side. The
Coroner's jury returned a verdict in
accordance with these facts. The Riley
woman was at one . time a resident of
Marysville.

boiler down, like a fallen steed, with its
nose in the dust. The tender was turned
almost upside down and was a wreck of
twisted iron and splintered wood. The
railway mail car was slightly careened
and stood diagonally across the track.
The express car was tilted over. The bag
gage car had turned a complete somer
sault and lav flat on Its side, ten feet
from the trackâ or what was the trackâ
for the ties were splintered and the steel
bent out of all shape. The first coach had
been hurled around so as to lie at right
angles with the track. The smoker was
slightly careened but not greatly dam
aged "The front truck of the first sleeper
had been torn off, but otherwise the car
was all right. .
As soon as the front cars jumped the
track and began to topple over the mail
clerks, express messenger and baggage
man began to break out of their cages of
death. The express messenger was A.
McNew; C F. Maltby was the baggage
man and the mail clerks were Scribner
and Prior. McNew had a gash over the
eve and Maltby's face was bruised slight
ly While these gentlemen were congrat
ulating themselves the engineer. Corne
lius Ford, and the fireman. Porter Woods,
were being scalded to death by the es
caping steam. Ford was pinned under the
toppled engine and lay there, suffering
indescribable agony until he was extri
cated by Conductor Bridges and others
who had" run to the assistance of the un
forunate man. He was carried in a
stretcher and placed in one of the day
coaches. The conductor said that Ford
uttered nothing intelligible, but others
say that he whispered that he did not
know anything was the matter with the
train until he felt the engine toppling
over That would tend to show that it
was not the engine that jumped the track
first In fact, it is quite well established
that one of the forward cars left the rails
first. _ , j, ... v.-
The fireman was frenzied with his
agony. He rushed from the track and
dashed through the reservation fence,
forcing his way through two rows of
barbed wire, and started to run like mad
in the open wheat field. Trainmen and
passengers caught him. tore his scalded
overalls and jumper from his body and
his gloves from his hands and placing him
on a stretcher conveyed him to one of the
sleepers.
There happened to be two trained nurses
on the train, one of whom was Mrs.
Kauffman. and they did everything pos
sible to relieve the sufferings of the en
gineer and fireman. Woods was conscious
to the last. Despite his excruciating pain,
he could not thank the kind women
enough. He talked continually about his
wife and boy. He said he hated to leave
them no better provided for.
Medical attendance was slow in reaching
the scene of the disaster. Dr. Wade from
Dos Banns Dr. Homestead from Newman
and a third doctor whose name the train
men did not remember finally arrived.
They were unable to do anything for the
pany. Owing to the absence of County
Surveyor Richardson action was post
poned" until to-morrow. The county's por
tion of the expense of building these three
sections is $1800, but there is no money in
the county general fund to pay the bill;
and it will have to go over until the latter
part of December, when the first install
ment of taxes is due. During this period
the amount will bear interest at the rate
of 5 per cent.
ARGENTINE PRESIDENT
RECEIVED IN BRAZIL
Assurances Are Given That No Ac-
tion Unfavorable to the United
States Will Be Taken.
RIO DE JANEIRO, Aug. B.â The Ar
gentine squadron, escorting President
Roca of the Argentine Republic, has
arrived here. F.etes lasting nine days
have been arranged in honor of Presi
dent Roca.
A Brazilian squadron went out to re
ceive the Argentine warships. The
President of Brazil, Dr. Ferra_ de
Campos Salles, on board the Rio
Chuelo, Inside the bay, received Presi
dent Roca and his suite in the presence
of the members of the Brazilian Cabi
net and a number of Brazilian generals
and Senators.
President Roca was then taken
ashore in a magnificent barge and
lodged in the presidential palace.
As to the political objects of the
visit, the correspondent of the Asso
ciated Press was assured to-day by a
high authority that if an attempt were
contemplated to ally the Latin-Ameri
cans against the United States it was
bound to prove a failure, for Brazil
will do nothing to offend America, her
best customer for her principal prod
ucts.
It is even doubtful, according to the
same authority, whether Argentina
will obtain a commercial treaty in
detriment to United States wheat and
flour, as Argentina already sells twice
what she buys from Brazil.
The chief advantage to be derived
from the visit of the Argentine Presi
dent is a harmonious understanding
aiming at a reduction of the military
expenses of the South American re
publics.

was dead, and that the names of twenty
seven other signers were not on the great
register. As the taking away of seventy
names rendered the petition without ef
fect It was thrown out, much to the dis
gust of a large number of anti-poolroom
men. who rose and left the hall.

DOING POLITICS
The Opinion of Embas
sador Choate.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LONDON, Aug. B.â Joseph H. Choate,
| the United States Embassador to Great
Britain, was interviewed to-day by a rep
; resentative of the United State 3in re
â  gard to the recent "war or arbitration"
i speech delivered by Sir Wilfrid Laurier,
I the Canadian Premier. He said: "There
j will be no war. It is safe to say that no
amount of political talk by politicians in
other countries could occasion war in this
connection."
The Embassador, continuing, said that
! the Canadian Parliament was on the eve
| of dissolution and the leaders of both par
] ties had to speak. Mr. Choate seemed to
j think that the latter circumstance cx
i plained Sir Wilfrid Laurier's position and
he was inclined to attach small impor
| tance to the Premier's remarks. Mr.
I Choate added that the negotiations were
i progressing, "But," he explained, "they
' are always slow and I can say nothing
at present as to their status."
Regarding Mr. Choate's statement the
! Canadian High Commissioner, Lord
Strathcona and Mount Royal, said to a
I representative of the Associated Press:
"Sir Wilfrid Laurier's remant was
! merely a figure of speech and not a po
litical dodge, as Intimated by Mr. Choate.
: Sir Wilfrid Laurier would not descend to
such a thing, nor did he in any way mean
!to challenge the United States. No sen
i sible man could regard war as possible,
1 but Canada is simply taking a leaf out
of the United States' book ln demanding
now what the United States demanded
In the Venezuelan case. Canada Is now
a nation, though closely allied to Great
Britain. As such she must protect her
rights. We merely ask for an equitable
settlement through arbitration, following
the United States' lead. We jelieve
President McKinley ls thoroughly in
earnest in his endeavor to have the Ques
tion honestly settled. The misinterpre
tation of Sir Wilfrid Laurier's remarks
by the United States Is unfortunate. Any
bitterness or abuse by the Canadian press
jis absolutely unauthorized. The negotia-
I tions are progressing and I believe a sat-
I isfactory settlement of the Alaskan
question will be achieved."

MEXICO TO
BLAME FOR
YAQUI WAR
Treacherous Treatment of
the Indians by General
Carbo.
OLD FEUD AWAKENS
Extermination of the Tribe Means
Disaster to the State of
Sonora.
By GUSTAV EISEN.
The seriousness of the Taqul war in
Sonora cannot possibly be overestimat
ed. The question at issue is not merely
if the. Yaquis are to be conquered or
not, but the whole welfare of the coun
try is at stake. If the war is continued
until the Yaquis are pacified or exter
minated dire disaster is sure to befall
that beautiful State. The extermina
tion of the Yaqui Indians simply means
the destruction of the manual labor In
Sonora. The Yaquis are not only the
best and most trusted workers In So 5
nora, but they constitute the largest
number of able worklngmen in that
State.
The Yaquis can In no way be com
pared with the Indians of this coast,
nor with any other Indians in the
United States, except, perhaps, the
Cherokees and a few other tribes
known for their peacefulness and for
their love of application to agriculture.
Since the very first settlement of So
nora by the Spaniards the Yaquis have
inhabited a small triangular territory
situated in the delta of the Y'aqui River
and extending from the Gulf to a place
inland called Buena Vista. A few Ya
quis are settled as far up as Comurifa,
but the principal Y'aqui country is as
just stated further down the coast. In
this territory the Y'aquis were found by
the Spaniards, and their tradition is
that here they have resided from im
memorial times. For centuries the
Mexican Government acknowledged
the right of the Y'aquis to live in and to
cultivate this territory and for centu
ries the Yaquis remained peacefully at
their work of cultivating the soil and
as general laborers elsewhere. Within
their territory the Y'aquis have even
now retained an independent govern
ment, with chiefs to decide according
to their laws and to mete out punish
ment to the guilty, and so perfect has
been their method of self-government
that the Mexican Government has had
no occasion to interfere. The Yaqui is
by nature moral and industrious and
no complaint can be made against him
on that account. All over Sonora there
are found Y'aquis in time of peace
working in the fields and in the mines
and even as laborers on the railroads.
In the latter capacity they are more
valued and more trusted than any
Mexicans, and we know of instances
where railroad agents have preferred
Y'aqui section bosses to those of other
nationalities.
As a laborer the Yaqui ia hard-work
ing and faithful and can always be re
lied on. He does not shirk his work
when his foreman turns his back and
he does not shorten his day's work by
continued cigarette smoking. No won
der, therefore, that he is highly valued
for the work he can and does perform.
As do many other laborers, he gets
drunk when pay comes on Saturday
evening, but he confines his carousing
to the rancheria in which he lives and
keeps his family and when Monday
morning comes around every man is at
his work.
The Yaqui country from Buena Vista
to the gulf has always been held as
common property by the Yaquis as a
tribe and has never been portioned out
to Individuals as in other communities.
The reason for this is found in the na
ture of the territory itself. The fer
tility of the Yaqui delta depends en
tirely upon the overflow of the Yaqui
River. In times of heavy rains the
delta and adjacent river bottom lands
are covered by water or made moist
by subirrlgatlon, while in dry seasons
the contrary is the case. As the ter
ritory is large, each Individual Yaqui
can always find a place suitable for
cultivation for that particular season.
Next season he may have to move to
another place in order to find moisture
and other conditions necessary. Now
it is evident that if the land were not
held in common and if every Yaqui had
his own allotted piece some would pos
sess suitable land for cultivation while
others would have dry lands, which
would be worthless unless properly ir
rigated.
All the recent trouble with the Yaquis
arises from this fact. The Mexican
Government wants the Y'aquis to divide
their land so that every Individual may
possess his own plat. This the Yaquis
refuse to do. The trouble originated
some years ago when under the presi
dency of Juarez the Mexican Govern
ment passed a law that there should
be no more public lands in the repub
lic, but that all lands so held should
be surveyed and sold. The same law
required every individual community
which possessed a "common" to have
the same surveyed at its own expense
and to have it divided and portioned
off, if not sold in a body for the good
of the community by which the "com
mon" was held. Thus in 1863 the town
of Hermosillo, now the capital of So
nora, had to survey its common and
dispose of it.
That this law was a most unjust and
illegal one no one can very well deny.
Suppose the United States Government
should pass a law that San Francisco
should divide up its Golden Gate Park
and its Yosemite reservation and give
every man a piece in order that he
might better and more undisturbed en
joy his share. What would be said of
such a law and how would Americans
stand such oppression? They would un
doubtedly object and the chances are
that they would forcibly resist any in
terference with their personal and pub
lic rights.
That is exactly what the Yaquis have
done. Indeed, so vested were the Yaquis
in the possession of their land that the
Mexican Government hesitated to ap
ply the law to their territory, but al
lowed them at least for the time being
to possess their land undisturbed.
While all other public lands in Mexico
were surveyed and distributed, the
Y'aqui country had remained intact for
about twenty years when the first
Y'aqui war broke out.
The outbreak of the war, which ex
terminated one-third of the Yaquis,
was, strange to say. not brought about
by the Mexican Government, but by
one of its generals in order to further
his own selfish ends. At that time
General Jose Guillermo Carbo was
commander of the federal forces in So
nora. Having illegally spent mony in
trusted to him for paying his troops,
he conceived the idea not only to
cover his peculations but also to bring
about a war in order to gain all he
could. In time of peace ever." general
is obliged to send in strict accounts to

THEM TO REVOLT

Russia Kill a Score of Peasants.
VIENNA. Aug. B.â The Neve Freie Presse to-day said the peasantry
of portions of Bessarabia (a government of Russia bounded on the
south by the Danube and the Black Sea) have revolted, being
driven desperate by famine. Troops, it is . added, have been sent there
and several encounters have taken place. A score of peasants have already
been killed and many have been wounded.

the contrary, he may draw* supplies
and money for expenses, and a arge
part may then find its way to^Hatt .
pockets without chance of discovery.
The only nation on which .could !
think of making war was - he Yaquis.
then partly living on their reservation
partly distributed over Sonora, zona
and even New Mexico as workers in
the fleld and in the mines. At that
time the Yaquis were governed by a
chief called Cajemi, an honest and
skillful man, generally esteemed and
against whom no one had ever had
! any complaints. General Carbo found
i as a pretext that Cajemi had not ful
filled the Mexican law regarding the
partition of the land and brought
against him other trumped-up charges
Without any right whatever Carbo
! appointed another chief over the
Yaquis, one who was an outlaw- .and
I outcast from his own tribe. Carbo
! promised this man every help if ne
could oust Cajemi and bring about as
! much disturbance as possible. This new
, chief and desperado got together a
small band of dissatisfied Indians and
without warning surprised cajemi s
wife and children and murdered them
: in cold blood. Cajemi himself was ab
sent from home at the time or he
would have, shared the fate of his
family. ' tf
Cajemi at once prepared to attacK
1 the marauders, when to his and every
body's astonishment he found them
i backed by the Mexican Federal Gov
ernment, through General Carbo.
Carbo put 3000 men in the field
' against Cajemi, but was signally de
feated in two open battles. The war,
! once begun, could not be stopped, and
j raged with more or less activity for
' fourteen years. Cajemi was at last
enticed into a palaver with Carbo, who
offered him safe conduct. Hardly had
, Cajemi shown himself before he was
captured and shot and some of his
principal men were slain with him.
This, of course, did not stop the war.
Other chiefs took up the strife, only to
' pay with their life for their foolhardi
ness. And so the war dragged on. It
: cost the Mexican Government millions
and millions of dollars and thousands
iof men. How many soldiers were killed
in the last war no one knows or ever
will know with any certainty, but a low
estimate gives the number as 1500 sol
â  diers laid low by Yaqui bullets every
year For fourteen years this would
'< mean over 20,000 men. The Mexican
Government was obliged to keep a
â  standing army at San Marcial of N be
tween three thousand and four thou
: sand men, and comparatively few of
these ever returned to the State from
', which' they came.
On the other hand the _aquis num
bered at the beginning of the war about
thirty thousand men. When in 189.
peace was made there remained only
ten thousand Yaquis.
The tactics of the Yaquis are to attack
suddenly and to ambush and Immedi
â  ately after the assault to run back into
the mountains, where no one can fol
low them. After a Yaqui has fought for
some time he suddenly becomes a
peaceful Indian and leaves the terri
tory In which the war rages. This hap
' pens when his ammunition is all spent:
he must then provide himself with
more. This he does by working In Ari
zona and New Mexico, as in these
places he can procure arms with his
savings and return when he has
enough. When he has accumulated
enough he returns by passing from
i Arizona through the Sierra Madre
wilderness, where he is free from sol
diers' bullets and from observing eyes,
i and when least expected he turns up
fully equipped with munitions to carry
OI The Yaqui is not the bloodthirsty
! beast that some reports have made him
; out to be. He is brave, industrious and
i peaceful; he does not torture his pris
-1 oner-* but neither does he allow them to
1 escape The Yaqui looks upon the na
i tive Sonoran as his friend and brother,
! and both Sonorans and Americans are
rarely the subject of his attack. But
the Mexican soldier, who is always
drafted from some distant state, is al
ways the especial object of his hatred
and considered as his mortal enemy
In 1897 peace was made with the
< Yaquis The Government promised
them a certain sum of money and they
in turn agreed to have their land sur
veyed and partitioned. During the
last two years the Yaquis have quietly
been working in the mines of Sonora
and Arizona and have saved their earn
ings in order to procure arms to renew
the war. Whatever will be the out
i come of the war, it will be a most ruin
i ous one for Sonora. If the Yaquis are
I exterminated, as the reports tell us
i Is the intention of General Torres, then
' the effect will soon be felt. It will mean
I the extermination of the manual labor
> in Sonora; it will mean the crippling
-. of her wheat and mining Industries.
'â  The Mexican Government could well
have afforded to allow the Yaquis to
' remain in possession of their land, as
their value as laborers ls many, many
time" greater than the price that can
be realized by selling their land. Sonora
i is a country with Immense resources,
I with enormous tracts of fertile soil
| where almost anything might be grown
i profitably. Such crops as wheat, sugar-
I cane beans, corn, oranges. dates,
peaches, apricots and many other fruits
are hardly surpassed anywhere. What
â  Sonora requires is not the Yaqui terri
' tory, but a development of its own Irri
! gation facilities.
The present Governor of Sonora Is
one of the most farseeing and enlight
ened and peaceful men ln Sonora, and
it is a great pity that the settlement of
the Yaqui trouble could not have been
â¢ left with the civil authorities instead
'of with the War Department. Sonora
can well afford to dispense with the
! Yaqui country, but It cannot afford to
j kill off the Yaquis.
MAYOS MAY JOIN
IN THE YAQUI WAR
â 
i Young Braves Are Dancing and a
Rising of the Tribe's Fighting
Men Is Feared.
AUSTIN, Tex., Aug. B. A dispatch
from Chihuahua. Mex., states that much
uneasiness is felt in military circles
there over the attitude of . the Mayo
I tribe of Indians in the State of Sonora j
I toward the Mexican authorities in the ;
| present uprising of the Yaqui braves.
' The fighting force of the Mayo triba Is
! much better than that of the Yaquis.
i and they have in the past for many
years been at peace with the Govern
ment. But the younger braves of -he j
tribe are now aroused over the war- j
fare' that is being waged just to the |
west of them and an open revolt is j
threatened. Dancing has been going j
on among the Mayos for the past three
| weeks and a rising may occur at any
moment.
The Mayos have long been friends of
the Yaquis, but they have held aloof
from them In' their previous wars
with the Government, which waged a
long and relentless war against them.
When the treaty of peace was finally
signed their numbers had been de
creased to a few* thousand.
The Mayos are almost white and are
of a higher order of intelligence than
the Yaquis. It is said the Yaquis sent
missionaries among the Mayos several
months ago to agitate a united and
concerted revolt against the Govern
ment. The ilayo chiefs refused to
sanction the uprising, but many of the
younger braves have caught the ex
citement and it is expected they will
be with the Yaquis in a body unless
their present plans are foiled by the
large force of Mexican troops in that
section.
Some of the members of the Mayo
tribe are wealthy. Their reservation
embraces many thousands of acres of
rich land in the valley of the Mayo
River.

ENTRAPPED AT BICAM
General Torres Will Bombard the j
Yaquis When His Artillery
Arrives.
CHICAGO, Aug. B.â A special to the
Record from Guaymas, Mex., says:
General Torres has almost complete
ly surrouunded Bicam, a Yaqui strong
hold, where nearly 2000 savages are
j massed. On the arrival of the artillery
and the machine guns the Mexican gen
; eral will close In on the Indians and
; precipitate the biggest battle ever
fought between the Government and
the Yaquis.
The reports from General Torres re
garding the killing of Remley and Mil
ler say their bodies were found hang
ing from a tree with the hands and
feet cut off. The uprising reaches down
the Yaqui River to the sea, and It is
not expected to extend further north
than the Sahuaripa district.
The Government now has nearly 5000
men In the field. Two hundred volun
teers will leave Guaymas for Medano
to-night on the steamer Jalisco.
A fight is expected, on entering the
Yaqui River, with a large band of sav
ages that last week attacked and
burned three sloops, A. Elres, a well
known Mexican at Hermosillo, losing
his life in the encounter.
Business at Guaymas and other
paints in Southern Sonora is practically
at a standstill in consequence of the
rebellion. It has not entirely suspend
ed the heavy freighting done with the
Yaqui Valley, but has led many Mexi
cans of the city to retreat beyond the
prefect's call for volunteers.
The man-of-war Democrata arrived
last night from Ensenada, Lower Cali
fornia, with the remainder of the Sev
; enth Regiment. The Democrata and
' the transport Oaxaca left to-day for
i San Bias "to meet the Sixteenth Regi-
I ment, which is en route from the City
of Mexico.
RECOMMENDS A GOLD
STANDARD FOR INDIA
Lord George Hamilton Says It Is the
Only Financial Safeguard of
the Country.
LONDON, Aug. B.â ln the House of
Commons to-day the Secretary of
State for India, Lord George Hamil
ton, in introducing the Indian budget,
said that after careful consideration
the Government has deliberately con
cluded that a gold standard was re
quired in the interests of India and
that they should not be deterred from
prosecuting this policy by all means ln
their power merely because the next
phase In the advancement was coinci
dent with abnormal or temporary fail
ure of the recommendation.
"If," continued the Secretary, "we
.could establish a great bank like the
Banks of England and France, it would
be*a fruitful ally in establishing a gold
standard."
Continuing, Lord George Hamilton
said he hoped the new century would
be associated with a series of financial
surpluses in India available for the re
duction of taxes, an Industrial develop
ment which would combine to give In
dia the fullest value of its association
with the cheapest, largest and most
fructifying market in the world.
The surplus, he said in conclusion,
this year will be 44,000,000 rupees.

WASHINGTON. Aug. B.â From the
character of the instructions sent to Gen
eral Brooke to-day it is evident that the
President and Secretary Root have been
giving serious consideration to the threat
ened San Domingo revolution.
It is probable, too. that this subject,
more than any other, is the immediate
cause of Secretary Root's visit to Lake
Champlain. Mr. Root received a sum
mons from the President just after sev
eral dispatches from General Brooke
about the suspicious movements of
Jiminez had been received at Lake Cham
plain. I am told that General Brooke
fears, from the operations of Jiminez.
that he is really planning for an e
tensive filibustering expedition from
Cuba. His frequent conferences with
other exiles from San Domingo have led
to this suspicion, and General Brooke
wired the department for instructions.
They were sent him to-day by direction
of the President, and they are to the ef
fect that he must prevent such an expe
dition at all hazards. ' ___...
According to the views of officials here
âand it is evident that President Mc-
Kinley and Secretary Root are of the
same "opinionâ the United States is just as
much obligated to prevent violation of
neutrality laws in Cuba as in the united
States. Although not American territory.
It is temporarily under the control of the
military forces of this country, and we
are responsible to the other nations of
the world to prevent any infraction of
international law by any one while in Cu
ban soil. _*"â "_â '"'â --.

republic in this country has already
called the attention of the authorities to
the contemplated expedition of Jiminez,
and he has been given assurances that
everything possible will be done to pre
vent any Violation of the neutrality laws.
HELD THE WHISKY
WITH A LOADED GUN
SAUSALITO. Aug. B.â An angry man
and a rifle formed a combination at
Mason's distillery, situated about a mile
north of town, which held a United States
Deputy Internal Revenue Collector and
two companions at bay and resulted yes
terday in a warrant being issued tor the
arrest of the belligerent distillery man.
It seems that P. Zimmerman, a whole
sale liquor dealer engaged in business on
Eighteenth and Shotwell streets, San
Francisco, purchased a quantity of liquor
from John Mason Jr., the proprietor of
the distillery, and the Government ware
house, and went there to take away the
liquor in company with W. F. Price, a
Deputy United States Internal Revenue
Collector, and George Raubinger. Price
released the whisky and Raubinger
started forward with the Intention of
loading it on a wagon. Previous to this
Zimmerman and Mason had a dispute
over their bargain, and, failing to come
to an amicable agreement, Mason warned
Raubinger not to touch the whisky. The
caution was disregarded, whereupon
Mason ran to his house for a rifle and.
returning just as Raubinger was in the
act of placing a barrel in his wagon,
pointed a gun at him and threatened to
kill him if he did not drop it. Raubinger
dropped the barrel and ran behind the
wagon, while Price and Zimmerman
scurried to a place of safety.
Raubinger came to town and swore to
a warrant for Mason's arrest en the
charge of assault with a deadly weapon.
Recorder Simpson Issued the wan ant,
which was served by Constable Garnty.

NEW YACHT FAR IN FRONT
LOSES ALL COMPETITORS BUT
THE DEFENDER.
Result of New York Yacht Club's
First Run Indicates That the
Shamrock Will Be Beaten
by the American.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
GREENPORT, L T.. Aug. B.â The first
run of the New York Yacht Club to-day
from Now London out Into the sound as
far a? Watch Hill and back to Gardlners
Bay was one of the most successful in the
club's history. Not only was the day an
Ideal one. with a great whole-sail breeze
from the west, which held through from
start to finish, but the club watched with
pride the magnificent run <".' the new
queen of the fleet, the Columbia, which
fairly distanced every other yacht with
the exception of the Defender, and she,
too, was defeated by four minutes and one
second in a thirty-mile race.
The old cup defender, Vigilant, was beat
en by the two fliers nearly an hour, but
much of this was no doubt due to the
fortunate run of the Columbia and the
Defender along the Fishers Island shore,
while the rest of the fleet kept over on
the other side of the sound. The Amorita
was in the schooner class.
Over fifty yachts crossed the line at
New London and nearly all finished In
Gardlners Hay, although some of them
did not get in until toward night.
The run gave the yachts a good broad
reach to Race Rock of three and a half
miles, a fine run before the wind to Watch
Hill of eight miles and a grand beat â¢of |
eight and a quarter miles in a constantly
increasing breeze to the finishâ an ideal
course all around.
By far the greatest event of the day !
was the magnificent seven-mile run of the |
Columbia from Fishers Island to i Great
Gull Island, during which she averaged
close to a thlrteen-knot gait. It was on |
this lee that the Columbia pulled out so
far on the Defender, for while the boats
were but a short eighth of a mile apart j
tinder Fishers Island there was nearly a j
mile and a half of water between them I
at Great Gull Island. Born yachts had j
all the wind they could stagger under, j
and the Columbia hauled down her jib j
topsail during a ten minutes' interval
when it blew the hardest. She set it up
again] however, when the Defender began j
to pick up; and held her advantage to the
line. Neither boat was held back a parti- j
cle, It was a good, square test of the'
merits of the old and new boats, and the j
latter may be put down as a good four
minutes faster under the present condi
tions, and with her steel mast in a couple I
of minutes more. â 'â 'â 
Once during the day, just after the
start, the Columbia seemed inactive and
lifeless and the Defender almost got by
her. But just before reaching Race Rock
the new boat seemed to find herself again
and from there she was never in danger,
while her great run on the last leg will
never be forgotten by those who witnessed
it. Summary of Columbia-Defender race:

ing 27 minutes 46 seconds behind the Col- j

the Sun. On reaching here the men were
intercepted by the pickets, who, after ex
plaining to them the nature of the con
troversy between the union and the Sun,
led them to the headquarters of the strik
ers, where they were well cared for.
They will be sent back to their respec
tive homes at the expense of the union.
President Delaney of "Big Six" this
afternoon said that not any of the strik
ers had returned to work in any of the
departments of the Sun.

OFFER EVIDENCE OF THEIR SKILL AS FISHERMEN.
RE. MILLER of the Owl Drug Company and Joseph McCowan of Livermore may relate tales of their prowess as
fishermen with impunity. They have the evidence to prove their case. During a vacation of one week spent at
Capitola they caught eighty-seven salmon, .pending in all fifteen hours with the rod. Their best catch for one day
0 was twenty-four fish, the largest weighing thirty-eight pounds and the smallest nine ounces. This is the banner
"catch of the season on Monterey Bay where rod and reel Is used. Like all fishermen who have had an opportunity
of enjoying this sport, they pronounce It fascinating and exhilarating. Since their return they have been advising their
friends* to go and do likewise.

I MONTREAL CYCLE RACES.
Noted Amateurs to Compete for the
Championship.
MONTREAL, Aug;, B.â Racing will be
gin to-morrow afternoon in the world's
bicycle meet. Prospects are for lino
weather, a big crowd and fast racing.
The Queen Park board track, built last
year, i. in the best of condition and ex
perienced riders like* Nat Butler and Ma
jor Taylor say they want no better. The
race of the day will be the one-mile ama
teur championship, and the list of en
tries Includes a. J. Cherry of London,
who holds the English championship;
John Caldow, the Scotch short distance
champion; Taillondiers, the Parisian
sprinter; Goodson, the Australian, who
holds all the amateur records of his coun
try, from a half mile to five mil'-s. Earl
Peabody of Chicago, George of South
Africa; Ingram, Moran, Carters, Brown
and Merrill of the League of American
Wheelmen circuit and a number of local
men. The race is expected to be between
Cherry, Tafllondiers and Peabody The
same riders are entered in the half-mile
amateur.
At the executive meeting of the Inter
national Cycling Association to-day it was
officially decided to recognize only the
League of American . Wheelmen in the
United States. Parle was selected as the
place for the world's meet next year.
President Batchelder of the N. C. A., In
an Interview to-night, said:
"The attitude of the N. C. A. in its
dealings with the international phase has
been more because of a desire to be
friendly with the Canadian Wheelmen's
Association than on account of any real
worth to it. We can easily afford to wail
another year if necessary for International
recognition, by which none will dispute
our position in the United States as the
controlling body in the East and Middle
East. Naturally our riders would like to
compete ln the world's events. It was not
I. C. U. representation we sought at this
time, simply the acceptance of the entries
of our riders pending the settlement of
the recognition question.'''

LOS ANGELES, Aug. The county

NEGRO SERGEANT IS
KILLED BY A SOLDIER
The Murderer Was Shot Down
While Attempting to
Escape.
SALT LAKE CITY, Aug. B.â First
Sergeant John Jackson was shot and
killed at Fort Douglass to-night by
Private W. H. Carter, and the latter
was then shot and instantly killed
while resisting arrest. Both are col
ored and belonged to the Ninth United
States Cavalry.
Jackson ordered Carter taken to the
guardhouse for Inattention to duty and
Carter was sent to his quarters for his
blankets. Instead of getting his blan
kets he got a Krag-Jorgensen rifle and
went to Jackson's room and shot him
twice, the last shot being fatal. After
Carter did the shooting he started to
ward the city in an attempt to escape.
A detachment of twenty-five soldiers
was ordered out to capture him. Car
ter shot several times at his pursuers,
but had not gone far when he was
fatally shot, his body showing seven
bullet holes. Both the men participat
ed In the battle of San Juan Hill. Jack
son enlisted at Culpepper Courthouse,
Virginia.

ery trap has made enormous cate'. es.

ASYLUM EMPLOYES TO
MAKE A LEGAL FIGHT
They Claim That the Board of Man
agers Cannot Dismiss
Them.
SAN JOSE. Aug. B.â The employes and
attendants of the Agnews Insane Asylum
i do not intend to relinquish their positions
i without. a legal fight and to protect them
â¢ selves have chipped in and engaged Sen
i ator H. V. Morehouse as counsel. The
recent investigation by the State Lunacy
Commission has stirred them up and ru
mors of wholesale changes have been cur
rent.
The Board of Managers held a meeting
to-night. A communication was received
from Senator Morehouse warning the
managers that they could not dismiss em
ployes or attendants at the asylum. He
stated that the board could only remove
the medical director and that the dis
charge of other employes lay with the
medical director, and then on"*,- after a
fair . and impartial trial. The Governor,
however, had the- power to remove the
managers. Senator Morehouse further
stated that this law was made in order
to remove asylums from the control of
politicians.
Things are not running .very smoothly
at Agnews. To-morrow the Investigation
into the treatment of a patient named
Iverson will come up. Iverson suffered
from a broken leg and It Is alleged that
Dr. Stocking was negligent in treating
him. Dr. Stocking is first assistant physi
cian and this Investigation is said to he
caused by Dr. Sponogle and his friends,
who are afraid that Dr. Stocking wilÂ» be
promoted to medical director.
It Is certain a new medical director will
be appointed as a result of Schaffer's In
vestigation. The candidates for the place
are numerous, among them being Dr. S.
P. Tuggle of San Francisco. Dr. C. 11
Freeman of Angela Camp, Dr. G. B.
Bowles of ikiah. Dr. Thomas R. Good
sin of Wllleta and Dr. J. F. Burns and
Dr. William Simpson of this city and Dr.
Marvin of Humboldt.
SOLD STOLEN WHEAT.
Woman Arrested on a Charge of
Grand Larceny.
DIXON, Aug. 8â Mrs. M. Ichtertz, a
farmer's wife living eight miles south
east of Dixon, and a farm hand were ar
rested yesterday on a grand larceny
charge for the theft of 259 sacks of wheat
from the farm of James Miller, a neigh
bor. They were taken to the County Jail
at Fairfield by Constable Staton this
morning to await their preliminary ex
amination. The grain was hauled to
Davisville. a distance of twelve miles, on
the nights of August 3, 4 and 5 and sold
at that place. The thieves wore tracked
by the officers, the grain located where it
had been sold and the arrests followed.
A Diminutive Baby.
SANTA ROSA, Aug. B.â What is in many
respects the most remarkable child ever
born in Sonoma County made Its appear
ance at the home of Mr. and Mrs. L. Mor
rison, near Windsor, on Wednesday.
Shortly after her arrival the little miss
was weighed, and fully dressed she tipped
the scales at Just a pound and a half.
Both the father and mother are very
proud of this little daughter. She is as
bright and active a youngster as one
would wish to see, and despite her dimin
utive size bids fair to soon overcome the
handicap placed against her by Dame Na
ture in the race of life. Two days after
her birth the little girl was comfortably
cradled ln an ordinary vegetable dish.
Changes at San Quentin.
SAN QUENTIN PRISON, Aug. B.â
Governor Gage proves an able second of
Warden Aguirre in the work of nepotism
now going on here. The howl over the
creation of a special office for Don Jose
Aguirre has scarcely died away, and yet
it is definitely learned that at Gage's be
hest Aguirre Intends in the near future
to appoint John Reaves of Los Angeles,
a brother-in-law of the Governor, to suc
ceed Victor ("Hardin as captain of the
lower gate. Reaves is now here and un
dergoing instructions in his duties from
Gllardin. It Is understood that Gllardln
is to be provided with another position.
Cooper Does Not Blame Montague.
Phil Montague says that in no manner
whatever did he attempt to cajole Harry
Cooper, who went his security for a bill
of printing with the Sunset Photo En
graving Company. Cooper, according to
his statement, agreed to stand good for
something less than $20 worth of goods
and not for any indebtedness which was
incurred later. While no limit was placed
upon Cooper's original agreement Mon
tague says that this was understood and
that Cooper holds him In no respect re
sponsible for the recent attachment
placed upon him. "The Sunset people,"
says Mr. Montague, "filed this attach
ment simply because of a little grievance
they had with Cooper. They know that
they will get their money from Bloom and
myself as soon as the books which they
finished for us are placed on the market;
but they are taking advantage of Cooper's
kindness to us to give vent to their own
personal spite against him. Cooper knows
this and holds us In no way responsible."
Nicaragua Canal Report.
At a. meeting of the trustees of the
Chamber of Commerce yesterday it was
decided to request President McKinley
to have printed the report of the Nicar
agua Canal Commission. While parts of
this important document have from time
to time appeared in the press dispatches,
the article In its entirety has never been
publicly presented. The members of the
local chamber desire to learn what was
gathered by the Investigation of the
commission of the proposed Isthmus
waterway. They believe that the report
would be read with interest by every
mercantile and commercial body .a the
United States. A resolution was passed
requesting the Secretary of the Treas
ury to make an Investigation Into the
San Francisco Bay quarantine station,
and especially its water supply,' with a
view to lmnr.o__ia._ie same. . Â

CALIFORNIA HORSE OUTFOOTS
FRANK BOGASH.
Driver Lockwood Protests Against
the Postponing' of a Race George
Carney Had at His
Mercy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BUFFALO, N. V. ( Aug. B.âSearch
light's three heats in the 2:04 pace at
the Fort Erie grand circuit meeting
this afternoon were the fastest ever
paced or trotted in Canada. Frank Bo
gash was not able, however, to crowd
the winner to the limit. Searchlight al
ways sold a heavy favorite In the pool
ing.
A big crowd turned out for the second
day of the meeting. Spear laid up Royal
Baron, the Merchant and Manufactur
ers' stakes winner, for two heats in
the 2:24 trot, the Electricity stake, and
then came on and outfooted Owyhee at
the wire for two heats. With the pools
selling $25 on the Baron to $5 on the
field they came out for the fifth heat.
Spear took the Baron far back and in
the stretch, although he was surely
catching the leader, one hundred yards
from the wire it looked like a fight. At
that time, however, the Baron left his
feet and galloped under the wire sec
ond. It was a big upset and Nick
Hubinger dropped a lot of money.
After three good heats ln the 2:20
trot the sun, at 7:10 o'clock, was still
streaming over the track, but the
judges decided to postpone the trot un
til to-morrow. This did not suit Lock
wood, with George Carney, who had
two heats and was beating his party
to pieces. He protested to no avail and
then, after the judges and spectators
left, he came out alone and did a mile
in 2:38 and claimed the race on the
ground that there was no occasion for
postponing the race and that the rules
sustained him. The judges to-night
said they would call Carney to-morrow
with the others, and if he did not show
up they would expel Lock wood.

SAD FRANCISCO
FAVORED WITH
A NEW OFFICE
J. P. Jackson Jr. Gets
the Job.
- â â¦ â
SECRETARY TO HIS FATHER
â â
COMPLIMENTARY APPOINTMENT
BY THE TREASURY.
â¦
New Berth Created by the Presiden
tial Civil Service Proclamation.
Salary Two Thousand
Dollars a Year.
Customs Collector Jackson is being con
gratulated by his friends upon the high
esteem in which he is held by the Secre
tary of the Treasury. A year or two ago
the Secretary appointed Stanley Jackson,
one "of the Collector's sons, to the im
portant position of Deputy Collector, at
a salary of" $3600 per annum, and only a
month ago the Secretary appointed an
other son, John P. Jackson Jr., as the
confidential clerk and secretary to Collec
tor Jackson, at a salary of $2000 per an
num. â¢
Five sons remain unappointed.
Collector Jackson modestly refrained
from informing the reporters on the Fed
eral detail of the good fortune which had
befallen him. The news leaked out un
officially in the usual Custom-house way.
"No," said the Collector yesterday when
asked about the matter. "I did not make
the appointment. The Secretary of the
Treasury made it. John has already as
sumed his official duties. He was ap
pointed in July." - Â'
One pleasant feature in connection with
the appointment in the fact that John I .
Jr. will not be overburdened with work.
Stanley Jackson is special deputy and
acts for the Collector and signs his name
in his absence. Knotty problems of Cus
tom-house law and of International
comity solve themselves automatically as
soon as they are brought within the po
tent influence of the official halo that
brightens the desk of Chief Clerk Jerome.
Stenographer Walter D. Hoyt does all the
official letter writing and typewriting for
.the Collector, so that John P. Jr. has a
clear field for his confidential duties.
The office is a new one, and was cre
ated by President McKinley in his re
cent civil service proclamation. By the
terms of that document Customs Collec
tors whose receipts in any year aggre
gate $500,000 or over are entitled to a con
fidential clerk and secretary, at a salary
of $2000 per annum. Owing to the creation
of a new dutyâ that of 10 cents per pound
on tea and the increased business caused
by the warâ the receipts of the San Fran
cisco Custom-house for the fiscal year
ended June 30, 1899, overflowed the $500,000
limit, and, therefore, made:
Customs Collector John P. Jackson
happy- _ , _ ,
Deputy Collector Stanley Jackson
happy. .â â '_ _ _
Confidential Clerk and Secretary John
P. Jackson Jr. happy.
ORGANIZATION OF
FUEL OIL EXCHANGE
MEETING OF MEN INTERESTED
IN THE INDUSTRY.
Wendell Easton Is Elected President
and He Appoints a Governing
Board to Engineer the
Project.
A meeting of representative men in
terested in the fuel oil Industry was held
: yesterday afternoon in the Merchants'
Exchange for the purpose of establishing
j an oil exchange for the handling of the
' State product, which is assuming large
! proportions owing to the many discoveries
of oil wells throughout the State. Thirty
j charter members were present and the
meeting was called to order by Wendell
Easton.
In outlining the plan of the organization
Mr. Easton stated that the fuel oil industry
| in this State was in its infancy and that
the supply was now being met by the de
! mand. As new properties were discovered,
however competition would become keen
and prices would go down. It was the. In
tention of the promoters to establish an
oil exchange similar to those now in op
eration In Pittsburg and London. The
exchange contemplated the selling of oil
under the certificate plan as now used in
other exchanges, which will assure fair
prices for the article. The meeting then
elected the following officers: President,
Wendell Easton; treasurer, Henry J.
Crocker; secretary, Emile Kahn.
The following committees were also
elected and will act as the governing
board of the exchange: Committee on
administrationâ Joseph Kahn, T. L.
Wooster, E. Gauthier Jr.; committee on
securitiesâ Werner Stauf. Leon Phipps,
Marion Leventrltt; committee on finances
âGeorge F. Beveridge, Louis F. Mont
eagle C. S. Benedict; committee on arbi
trationâJesse W. Llllenthal, Fred W. Me-
Near, George A. Moore.
The meeting adjourned to meet next
Tuesday afternoon at the Merchants' Ex
change, when further plans for the or
ganization will be outlined.
Municipal Gas Plant.
SAN JOSE, Aug. S.â Santa Clara, which
is a model municipality, has decided to
erect a city gas plant. The town already
owns its own waterworks and electric
lighting plants. The Board of Trustees
has called a special election for Wednes
day, September 20. for the purpose of vot
ing for a bond issue of $30,000 to erect a
gas plant. Five per cent premium -s ex
pected on the bonds. Plans and specifi
cations have been prepared for the ga3
plant, and It is expected to have it in
working order before the end of the year.
