FAREWELL TO
THE OREGON
VOLUNTEERS
Away for Home and
Civil Life.
SPEEDING PARTING GUESTS
KISSES FOR THE COMMANDER,
CHEERS FOR THE MEN.
Payday at the Presidioâ A Month's
Money Is Distributed to
Every War-Worn
Veteran.
The Oregon Regiment has gone home,
' the cheers of the city's farewell still
i ringing in the ears of its men aid the
' soldiers' throats still dry from the lusti
ness of their good-bys. They went in a
body, not all from the Presidio as had
been the intention, but they were all at
the ferry depot, and there the regiment
trooped on board the 3 o'clock boat, men.
women and children following after and
all loth to leave or be left behind.
It had been announced to the men of the
regiment before they left camp on Mon
day that they should all march away from
! the Presidio yesterday at 1 o'clock. It
' was not an order, for by 1 o'clock yester
< day the regiment was no more, but it
was an appeal to the men. Human nature
1 could not stand the strain, however, and
: when 1 o'clock came there were not over
100 men at the camp, and a good propor
i tion of these were officers. They were
'â  formed in line by General Summers, how
ever, and with the parting admonition,
i "take plenty of intervals," the men moved
! out of the "post after the Utah men and
: the Nebraska band, who had volunteered
1 to escort them as far as the ferry.
The officers marched together, four
! abreast and after them came the men.
1 There was the involuntary step of the
: trained soldier and the even pace and
1 straight lines and the uniforms, but there
; the military element stopped. The men
: were too happy to keep silence In the
ranks and they did not have to, anyway,
so they laughed and chatted to them
selves and to the crowds which gathered
! and they spoke to friends and called to
! strangers and had a glorious time gen-
I erally all the way to the water front.
The band played its prettiest ana the
artillerymen marched their best, but then
! they were still soldiers, not citizens, as
were the men they led. And It was bet
ter that way perhaps. One was the vol
unteer In service; the other the volunteer
mustered out, and the difference between
â  them told the story of the war and why
there need never be a standing army. The
Utah men will soon march down the street
as gay and happy and light-hearted as the
| Oregon men, and some other waiting regi
ment will walk as sedately and as sol
dierly before them.
On the way down the line of Oregon
men grew larger, for some were waiting
for it at every crner. They just tagged
|on behind, for there was no company
; formation, just a simple column of fours:
no commanding officer, just an old friend
i at the head of the line, and the line kept
! itself with nothing but the habit gained
in months of hard drill to direct it. They
| marched without incident except a few
! displays of enthusiasm by some women,
â¢ who to show their Interest in the regi
ment rushed from the crowds and Insisted
I on kissing General Summers.
At the ferry there were at least 600 men
awaiting the line from the Presidio. They
began coming before 2 o'clock, and before
the boat left at 3 they crowded the ferry
! depot above and below. The parade
! reached the water front In good time for
; the 3 o'clock boat, and after good-bys and
; waving of hats and handkerchiefs, and
: cheers and smiles and tears, the men
boarded the boat and were borne away.
At the Oakland mole their train was
waiting for them. It was in three sec
tions. The first got away soon after the
men reached the mole, and then the oth
ers slipped out after It, and before night
the last of the Oregon men were on their
way home.
It was payday in the volunteer camps
at the Presidio yesterday, and as a conse
quence the Utah. Nebraska and Pennsyl
vania men stayed in camp until that inter
esting ceremony was over. Each man
l drew pay for the month of June.
Colonel Girard, commandant of the gen
i eral hospital at the Presidio, is preparing
! to discharge all men able and willing to
be discharged under the recent permis
sion of the department allowing the dis
charge of men before their regiments
come home. He will discharge between
I thirty and forty men at .once. *
The sailing of the transport Morgan
: City has been set for to-morrow, Au
gust 10.
Lieutenant B. H. Dorcy, Fourth Cav
alry, has been assigned to the transport
Leelanaw as quartermaster and commis
sary. The Leelanaw will sail August 14
with 250 horses. The Slam will sail on.
the same date. She also will be loaded
, with horses. Captain Joseph P. O'Neil
will sail on the Stam as quartermaster
, and commissary.
First Lieutenant Andrew E. Williams.
Third Cavalry, has been assigned to the
! transport St. Paul as quartermaster and.
j commissary. The St. Paul is expected to
! sail August 14 with 150 recruits for the
' Third Cavalry.

; leave of absence for a month has been
granted Second Lieutenant Hiram A.
Piatt, First South Dakota Volunteer In
-1 fantry. A month's leave has also been
granted to Second Lieutenant Henry M.
Merriam, Third Artillery.
Acting Assistant Surgeons Thomas C.
Stunkard, William H. Tukey and Henry
H. Bradley have been ordered to report
for duty to the general hospital at tho
, Presidio.
The remains of the late Colonel Haw
; kins will lie in state at his home in Penn
sylvania until his regiment is mustered
out in this city and returns to Pennsyl
vania, then the body will be interred with
full military honors. â .*â .,*â¢â 'â .

! eral companies of the Pennsylvania Reg
iment as to whether they would return
'to their State as a regiment. The result
; was unanimous In favor of such a course.CROCKER GIVES

MECHANICS' INSTITUTE FAIR.
Preparations for San Francisco's Big
Show Are Well Under Way.
The trustees of Mechanics' Institute
held a special meeting last evening to con
sider matters pertaining to the annual fair,
which will open in the pavilion on the
night of September 2. It was decided that
the opening exercises should be held In
the pavilion on the opening night, instead
of in some downtown theater, as formerly.
A communication was received from tlie
Regents of the State University to the ef
fect that they had appropriated $200 for
the university exhibit. A letter was also
received from Mrs. Phoebe Hearst offering
to place on exhibition the plans for the
contemplated improvements at the univer
sity after they have been passed upon ny
the Judges.
The work at the pavilion is progressing
rapidly, and the Filipino actors will begin
training for their exhibit at once. All the
Filipinos are in good health with the ex
ception of the baby born a few days ago,
which is suffering from some infantile ail
ment.

FROM THE FIFTH STORY
WALTER LTON committed suicide in a spectacular manner yester- â¬
day' day moming by plunging from the fifth story of the Donohoe build- 4
ing on the corner of Market and Taylor streets to the street below c

ander at 1046 Folsom street. He had formerly worked in the sugar mill of <
Louis Saronl's candy manufactory, but was obliged to leave that position ,
because of sickness. <

Having been -.uttering with an incureble DESESE untill driven almost Insane I have
at last resolved to end It all. Respect fully, Â«_ -** 1 " *
Thomas Tobinson. assistant -janitor of the building. while making his 1
rounds a half past 5 o'clock yesterday morning discovered that room No 87 ,

man Patrolman O. B. Merrick appeared and the three men went to the .

">lv friend" said the officer, "you are in a dangerous place. You d 1

AFFECTATION
UNDERMINING
RARE TALENT
MO one who makes theater going |
a habit can tail to note the ,
awful epidemic of voice affecta
tion that is sweeping over the J
dramatic land. Scarcely a the- ,
! ater in San Francisco to-day but offers
I the sad spectacle of at least one young j
woman of talent rapidly working herself
into the idle ranks of the profession by
the cultivation of certain false methods
of delivery. The affliction generally takes
the form of tricks of speech, faults
mainly gathered from the star, to whom
they "arc natural and consequently tol
erable though not admirable, and given
to a suffering audience in various degrees
of perversion. Nor are the men far be
hind In the race for dialect supremacy.
In fact, I think they are winners. And
these are young men and women of m
tellflencl, too, and so occupied are they
with the 6 ' absorbing â  business of making
Their dialogue almost unintelligible to
everything back of the fourth row that
' thly start out with one pitch at i 8:15 and
' never alter It until 11. when It is to be
: hoped they recover their right minds
and their natural tongues, thus showing
to their friends a mercy denied to us.
i This monotony of pitch is sometimes ag
gravated by a monotony of strength and
i _ long dialogue of many shades of senti
-1 ment is delivered as though the language
of a cook book were being "steered into
, the ears" of a multitude of deaf
'â  "The 1 musical beauty of a well modulated,
unaffected delivery cannot be too strongly
emphasized as the only worthy ambition
of an intelligent tongue, and if we are
not in time to depend entirely upon panto
mime for our Information, for mercy s
: lake let the misdirected energies of these
talented people be diverted into their
Proper channels. Elocution is not to be
i recommended, but a season of distinct,
,' cfearf utterance would be a delightful re
! laxation after the tension of so much con
j centrated trying to hear what has not
â¢ been said. In a criticism of play and
! players everything cannot be -noted, but
i silence on the subject need not be mis :
taken for "winking at delinquencies.
I The error of their course is calculated in
I time to be very profitable to the waiting
student, for these mistaken people are
THE SAN FBANCISCO CALL. WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 9, 1899.
rapidly mouthing on to their last public
speech. Too much toleration is fatal, and
given lips, tongue, teeth, palate and a
diaphragm in normal conditions the "in
articulate genius" is unpardonable.
CHARLOTTE THOMPSON.
Tivoli's New Prima Donna.
Long after the echoes of the present
I Tivoli grand opera season have died away
I there will remain in the memory of those
I who attended "Faust" last evening a
| Marguerite the like of which is but rarely
I found. You may have heard the role ren
i dered by vocalists of wider reputation
1 than that of Mme. de Pasquali. you may
have succumbed to the fame of one or the
other Marguerites, but it is an indisput
able fact that you have never heard it
sung any better. Mme. Pasquali's so
prano is so caressing, so pure, that it
i rings in your ears like the dying sound of
j a silver bell. She phrases with an exacti
| tude and care that bring every note into
bold relief, and, most of all, she sings
j with so much taste and warm color and
i a temperament that rouses enthusiasm.
! But one seldom has an opportunity to see
'< Marguerite acted as well as sung and it
la exceedingly gratifying to note that
i Mme. de Pasquali makes the histrionic
part of Marguerite as important as the
musical.
In view of the fact that Mme. de Pas
quali has hitherto devoted most of her
time to concert work it Is but fair to state j
j that after such a tremendous success she j
! should remain on the operatic stage. The j
! Jewel song was a masterpiece of first i
t grade and received two most enthusiastic
encores. In the trio she became even dra
matic and in no instance did she forget >
that she had to be actress as well as :
! singer. Mme. de Pasquali has a brilliant ;
future. She is a native of Boston and !
; studied in New York. This is a striking ,
i example of the fact that America -can
produce great singers without the assist- I
: ance of Europe. _MK"f_f

VIGILANCE SOCIETY FATHERS
THE MOVEMENT.
Mrs. John Pettee Calls a Meeting of
Clerks at Golden Gate Hall and
Proposes Organization of
Clubs.
The Vigilance Society for the Preven
tion of Cruelty to Women, of which Mrs.
â  John Pettee Is the president, held a meet
i ing last night at Golden Gate Hall tor the
purpose of organizing clubs among the
clerks of San Francisco and thus force
their employers to close" their stores on
' Saturday nights. Music by a mandolin
orchestra and an occasional song ' were
rendered to relieve the monotony of the
evening.
M. M. Foote in an opening address
stated the reason for the meeting and the
Issuance of 5000 circulars to the clerks.
What he wanted was the protection of
the young girls who are obliged to work
for their living, and he exhorted all to
lend a helping hand.
Airs. John t'ettee made a lengthy speech
in which she berated the clergy for their
lack of interest in the movement. She
said that the stores should close at fi
o'clock, and suggested a refusal of pa
tronage if no other means should prove
efficacious. Committees will be appointed
to go the rounds and state the proposi
tion to storekeepers. The labor unions
will also be asked to join in the move
ment,
EPWORTH LEAGUE RALLY.
Delegates to Indianapolis Convention
Tell of Their Experience.
A well-attended mass meeting or rally
of Epworth Leaguers, for the purpose of
welcoming home the Central California
delegates to the recent International con
vention at Indianapolis and to listen to
their reports on the result of their suc
cessful efforts to bring the convention of
1901 to San Francisco, was held in How
ard Street M E. Church last night.
Rev. Dr. John A. B. Wilson, pa_ior of
; the church, made a few remark's and In
troduced Rev. Dr. Case, who presided,
Interesting addresses were made by Dr.
! Case, Dr. Filben, Dr. Beard, Brothers
I Hutchinson. Waste. Norton and several
1 other delegates, the addresses being lib
j erally Interspersed with singing by the
I audience.
Preliminary steps were also taken to
i arrange for the holding of the next con
vention ln San Francisco.
Freight traniferred at low rates. Signal
Transfer Co.. 530 Jones; tel. Butter 441.
QUEER FEATURES
IN THE CASE OF
A DIPSOMANIAC
Death of Mrs. Dr.
Dunn of Fresno.
FOUND DEAD IN HER HOTEL
HAD MADE SEVERAL ATTEMPTS
AT SUICIDE.
Was the Wife of a Prominent Physi
cian, but Could Not Be -
Cured of Her Fatal
â  Habit. â¢ [ 'tfy : tf
**
Mrs. E. C. Dunn, wife of a prominent
physlclan of Fresno, was found dead in
bed In a room In the Grand Hotel about
7 o'clock last evening. Whether or not she
was a victim of self-destruction or wheth-
er death was due to natural causes re-
mains to be determined.
Mrs. Dunn engaged a room during the
afternoon. She told the bell boy, who hud
known her for some time, that she was
not feeling well, and asked to be called
at the hour of 7. The servant advised the
woman to take a good sleep, and she
promised to do so.
At the appointed hour the required call
was made, but there was no response. It
was finally found necessary to force the
door, and Mrs. Dunn was found dead. A
flask of whisky, part of the contents of
which had been consumed, was found on
the table, and a towel had been hung
over the keyhole to prevent any one look-
Ing Into the room. There was no note or
MRS. E. 'C. DUNN.
anything to indicate that the woman had
committed suicide. , :, â
The remains were Immediately removed
to the Morgue. Fulton G. Berry of Fres
no, who happened to be stopping at the
hotel, took the case in hand and at once
arranged to have the body taken to a lo
cal undertaking establishment. He also
telegraphed the distressing news to Dr.
Dunn in Fresno, who announced that he
I graphed in this city this morning.
in in Fresno, who announced that he
dd arrive In this city this morning,
â¢The deceased had for several years been
a dipsomaniac, and everything possible
had been done by the husband to cure her,
but in vain. She had been here several
months under the care of physicians, out
could not conquer her habit. After in
dulging in liquor she would become mel
ancholy and develop the suicidal mania.
The hapless woman had made several at
tempts to take her life, the latest being a
week ago last Sunday, when she tried to
jump off the Fourth-street wharf. A by
stander interfered and Mrs. Dunn was
taken to the Central Station and locked
up for the night. During her incarcera
tion she asked for a hatpin, with which
to kill herself, and later attempted to tear
open the artery of her arm with her teeth.
On this occasion she was saved by a cell-
I mate. About a year ago Mrs. Dunn took
' - large dose of morphine, and when her
husband discovered the fact she begged
him to let her die and end her misery.
Deceased was a woman of fine presence,
hlehiv popular among her friends and had
the sympathy of all who knew of her
weakness. At the time of her death a di
vorce suit brought by her husband, was
pending. The ground stated for the-ac
tion was intemperance. . - â 
FRESNO Aug. S.-The report that Mrs.
Svbll Dunn' had committed suicide caused
no Teat surprise among her friends in this
city Those who knew her best, including
her husband. Dr. E. C. Dunn, felt re
lieved when they heard that the unfortu
nate woman had at last succeeded in end
ing her strange and wayward life.

he tearfully spoke . of his wife's fatal
craving for alcohol and it life blight

to 'say anything further for publication.
When he sued Mrs. Dunn for divorce he
declared that he did so . not because he

pelled to protect himself against further

DIED ON THE STREET.
Mrs. Mamie Cusick Found Lifeless in
a Doorway.
Mrs. Mamie Cusiek. a woman about 26
years of age, was found dead in a door
way at 1646 Market street about half-past
6 o'clock last evening. The cause of her
death is yet a mystery, but it is the opin
ion of many persons who knew her Chat
she succumbed to natural causes, the re
sult of overindulgence- in liquor. tf-i'o
Pedestrians on Market street found the
unfortunate woman crouched in the door
way and summoned Officer T. F. Conno- l
lv The policeman, thinking that life
might possibly not he extinct, called a
patrol wagon and had her driven to the i
Receiving Hospital. Upon her arrival
there it was found that she was dead and
the Coroner's office was notified. Depu- j
ties Flaimigan and La Combe conveyed I
the .body to the Morgue.
Mrs Cusiek a husband was a paper- '
hanger in this city and has been dead one j
year. Since then the woman has been i
working in families and drinking exces- I
sively at times. Her father resides in St. I
Helena and her mother is dead. Mr.-. Ellen
Cusiek. the mother-in-law of .the dead
woman, resides at' 6S Herman street, at
which address the latter has recently i
made her home at intervals.
An i-iouest will be held on the remains I
to determine the cause of death. Nothing
was found on the body which would pro
duce death and the only theory at pres
ent is that the unforunate woman was the.
victim Â°- drink. . _*. â ;

A Soldier Insane.
Fred Miller, a discharged soldier of
Company F, Third Infantry, went violent
ly insane at ' the corner of Market and
Third streets last night about S o'clock.
He was taken to the Harbor Receiving
Hospital. Miller was discharged from the
army for reason of disability.

QUICK RUN FROM AUSTRALIA
CITY OF SYDNEY CHARTERED
BY THE GOVERNMENT.
Machinist on the Relief Burned With
Ammonia and a Negro Cut .
With a Hatchet by a
Bootblack. .
â -''.-. â¢ Â»_â  '
j The steam schooner Noyo did consiaer
â¢ able damage at Mission street wharf yes
\ terday. She was coming in deeply laden
; with lumber, and before the headway
could be stopped the flood tide took con
trol and the steamer crashed. Into the
, wharf. About eight feet of the stringers
, " an'_ planking were torn up and one of tne
shipping offices on the wharf was knocked
: over. The steamer was not damaged very
much and the shock of the collision was
! not felt to any extent by the passengers,
j The bark Colusa arrived in port from
, Kahului yesterday with a list to star
! board. She has aboard 30,150 bags of sugar
; and some of it has been damaged. On
; August 5 and 6 she was caught In a strong
! northeast gale. A very heavy sea fol
lowed, which caused the vessel to labor
i and finally shift cargo to starboard. The
j Colusa has not been ln port for a long
: time, as she has been in the lumber trade
! between Puget Sound and Australia-
' The French bark Jacques arrived from
Newcastle, Australia, in the good time of
sixty-three days yesterday, and was fol
lowed into port a few hours later by the
1 American bark General -*airchild. in a
passage of eighty days. The French
barks Jacques, Bretagne and Dugesclin
, all left Newcastle, Australia, for San
1 Francisco on the same day, while the bark
Lamoriciere sailed a week ahead of them.
Each captain expected to be the first to
reach this port, but Captain Bernadac
beat them all and at the same time made
the best run of the season.
There was trouble on the ship Standard
' yesterday. The tug Rescue was alongside
ready to tow her to Portland to load for
Australia, when at the last moment Cap
| tain Backus refused to go out in the ship.
j There was a hurried consultation, and
I about 4 p. m. a lot of extra supplies were
! put aboard. Finally, the matter was ad
justed and the ship was towed to sea.
â¢ The steamer City of Sydney was char
tered by the Government from the Pa
cific Mail Steamship Company yesterday.
She will be got ready at once to carry
troops to Manila.
Hugh McCreedy. a machinist on the
hospital ship Relief, met with a peculiar
i accident yesterday. He was working on
; a valve of the Ice machine, and thinking
all the ammonia was out of the reservoir
â  he was not as careful as he might have
| been. Suddenly the stopcock blew out
and struck him a severe blow on the chest
that doubled him up. As he bent forward
I the ammonia spurted out of the open vent
| and struck him full in the face, burning
him very badly. He was treated aboard |
the Relief.

j Harbor Commissioners Meet, i
At the regular meeting of the Harbor
Commissioners yesterday afternoon Chief
Engineer Holmes submitted plans and
specifications for the construction of the
Santa Fe terminal, car ferry and freight
slip, at the foot of Main street. The plans
were adopted by the board, and Secretary
English was directed to advertise for bids.
The Santa Fe officials were invited *o
meet the Commissioners in their office on
J Thursday at 11 o'clock to express their
views as to when they desire the slip com
pleted.
The approach to the slip is to be 400 feet
I long, and an apron. 100 by 24 feet, will be
i worked by hydraulic power and a coun
terpoise weighing 100,000 pounds. The
bridge proper will weigh 200,000 pounds In
all. It is thought that the slip can be
completed by January 1. 1900.
Informal bids will be asked for the lay
ing of a bituminous pavement in front
of the ferry building in anticipation of
the return of the California Volunteers.
The sewers now* being constructed on
Dupont street were ordered to be* contin
ued into the bay across North Point
street, which is State property, at a cost
of $1500. ;
M. Cordes addressed the board on the
| subject of eucalyptus piles, and will dis-
I tribute eighty of them along the front for
! experimental purposes.
Captain Monroe of the steamer Pro
greso, which ran into Main-street wharf
recently, sent a cheek for $35 to repair
the damage, as he considered himself to
I blame. The captain's voluntary action
excited the favorable comment of the
j members of the board. ; ;-tftf .â ,-. .-.--,*,

I BABY WAS SMOTHERED.
Ethel Ward, One Month Old, Killed
by Its Two-Year-Old Brother.
Ethel Ward, an infant one month old,
was accidentally suffocated by its 2-year
old brother last Monday night at the resi
dence of its parents, 1211 Steiner street. !
The children slept together with their
parents in one bed. the 2-year-old sleeping
|at the foot. At a quarter after 7 o'clock
yesterday morning, when the parents
awoke, they found the 2-year-old boy ly
ing fast asleep on the baby's head. The
baby was dead. The little boy during the
night had crawled up to sleep beside Its
parents and had accidentally smothered .
his baby sister. _."*__ , >
The parents are verj* poor but have nine
children living out of eleven born- ,

PIONEERS' REUNION.
Surviving Passengers of the Old
Panama Meet in the Bohe
mian Club.
The surviving passengers and crew of
the ship Panama, which arrived in San
Francisco Bay fifty years ago (August S,
1*49), were the guests of George D. Dor
nin at the Bohemian Club last evening.
There were present William H. Bod
fish, son of the captain; ex-Supervisor
William H. Hilton, capitalist. Berkeley;
Harry N. Morse, of the Morse Detective
Agency and George D. Dornin, Insurance
n*anag"er. George W. Dornin, a Native
Son, was also a guest. >â â '-:. -.â  tf
A reminiscent letter, excusing himself
because of illness, was read from Henry
Williams of the H. A. Williams Company
on Market street, â  Ctftftf- -'.-â¢.
The ship Panama, Captain Russell S.
Bodtish, sailed from New York on Feb
ruary 3. 1549. and after a voyage of IS6
days, reached San Francisco on August S. ]
Her passengers and crew numbered 220
souls. â â * '-.."'â  â 'â¢".-'-â¢" :"
Soon after her arrival the Panama was
sold to Macondray & Co. and utilised as ;
a storehouse. Subsequently she was sold !
to the Seamen's Bethel Association, :
hauled up on the flats in the neighbor- j
hood of the present Sacramento and Da- |
vis streets and converted into a church,
the space between decks being utilized
for church purposes. A more pretentious i
superstructure was placed upon her sub- j
sequently. She was burned in one of the I
Â«arl** fires.

COURTING TRADE.
Manufacturers', and Wholesalers'
League to Entertain Their
Guests.
The officers of the Manufacturers' and
Wholesalers' League are highly pleased
with the progress made by the members
in perfecting plans for inducing buyers
to make their purchases In this city in
stead of sending orders for goods to the
East. As has been previously stated, it.
is necessary to offer some inducement to
the country merchants to make the de
sired change, and this is what the
league is doing. In the first place efforts
will be made to have buyers come to the
city and deal with the wholesalers direct.
One important feature is similar to that
practiced by the wholesalers of some of
the Eastern cities, offering transportation
to and from the city at greatly reduced
rates.
Several of the railroads of the Coast
States have offered rates that will make
transportation very cheap. More than
â¢this, the rates will apply to the families
of the prospective buyers. The officers
of the league yesterday received a com
munication from the proprietors of the
best hotels in the city stating that they
would make reductions in board and lodg
ing during the stay of-the visitors from
the country. The-managers of the thea
ters also agreed to entertain those In
dorsed by the league at special prices.

Mf-IKE IT A PUBLIC HOLIDAY.
rROM tlie degree of popular interest already dis
played in the work of preparation for the wel
come of the California volunteers it is evident
that on the occasion of their arrival in the city the
people will be satisfied with nothing less than a pub
lic holiday. All classes of patriotic citizens will desire
to participate in the reception, and it is but right they
should have an opportunity in the freedom of a gen
eral holiday to do so.
While the date upon which the volunteers will ar
rive is not known and cannot be definitely determined
at this time, the arrangements which have been made
ior the reception are such as will allow the Governor
ample time in which to issue his proclamation for a
holiday on the date when the troops will land. Ac
cording to the plan adopted for the reception the
transports will arrive at the Faraliones at noon, and
the news of the arrival will be at once dispatched to
every locality in the State. If the Governor then act
promptly in declaring the following day a legal holi
day, the information can be received in time to be
acted upon throughout California-
It is right and fitting that such a holiday should be
declared. Californians have a just pride in the record
of the volunteers, who were the first to respond to the
call of the President for the war with Spain. In the
Philippines they distinguished themselves for valor
and discipline even in that army of ardent and heroic
men. Having been the first of the volunteers to enter
the service they are among the latest to be discharged.
.They turn to us, therefore, with every honor that
accrues to those who have faithfully performed the
dangerous duties of a soldier's life, and it is but nat
ural all citizens should feel a desire to join in the
throngs that will welcome them home.
The preparations that have been made by the com
mittee in charge of the reception are of a nature that
w ill fully serve for the demonstrations ot a public
holiday. The decorations of the city by day, the il
luminations at night, the parades, the receptions and
the other festivities will suffice to make the occasion
one of the most festal in our history.
â¢ The number of persons directly interested in the
occasion, including the relatives and friends of the
volunteers, the Native Daughters and the Native
Sons, is so considerable that every portion of the
community and all classes of citizens are within the
sphere of their influence. All, therefore, will desire
the freedom of a holiday in which to join in the cele
bration. A widespread, popular demand of this char
acter ought to be gratified. All business and labor
should be suspended for the day. Every clerk and
every workingman â all employers and employes â
should have the day free for the untrammeled delight
of welcoming the heroes home.
STREET PAVING .SUGGESTIONS-
WITH the current number 'of the Merchants' As
sociation Review the third anniversary of the
association is noted, but no adequate summary
is given of the work accomplished for the year. The
neglect was doubtless due to the amount of space re
quired to publish reports dealing with important is
sues of our municipal government.
The leading place in the review is given to the re
port of the committee upon the tax levy for the year,
a subject which has been so fully discussed by the
press as to render further comment unnecessary, par
ticularly as the levy has now been made within the
dollar limit recommended by the association.
The discussion of street paving is continued from
the July number, and the experience of Eastern cities
is cited for our guidance. The report is made by L.
M. King, superintendent of the association, and is
based upon personal investigation of street paving in
the East.
Of our bituminous rock pavements it is said they
are unsatisfactory for three causes first, they are
composed of a natural mixture and therefore not of
uniform quality; second, they are not laid in the best
manner; third,, they are not kept in repair, and when
repairs -ire made the work is frequently poorly per
formed. It is stated that if a binder course were laid
over the concrete, thus securing firm adhesion be
tween the foundation and surface coat, and the pave
ment were rolled with heavy steam rollers imme
diately after being laid and while hot, much of the
waviness and uneven surface would be avoided.
Of the basalt block pavements it is said they should
be laid on concrete foundations, and the joints filled
with gravel and asphalt or coal tar cement. It is main
tained, however, that we should no longer confine
ourselves to the use of bituminous rock, basalt blocks
on sand and cobbles, but make use of asphalt, vitri
fied brick and asphalted wood.
The conclusions, which are the same as those re
commended by President Dohrmann, are as follows:
'.'First â Admit asphalt, vitrified brick and asphalted
wood block pavement under proper specifications.
Second Change the specifications for bituminous
rock paving so as to require the use of a binder, and
also that the pavements shall be rolled while hot with
steam rollers. â Require that all business streets
when repaved with basalt blocks shall have the block**
laid on a concrete foundation and shall be grouted
with gravel and asphaltic cement. Fourth Arrange,
if possible, so that all pavements shall be guaranteed
for ten years with good surety bonds. Fifth â the
work of repairing the basalt block and cobble streets
shall be done systematically. Sixth That the city it
self undertake the work of making repairs over
trenches in the pavement made by corporations and
plumbers. Finally â Do away with the prevailing idea
that cheapness is the main consideration. Good work
costs money, but, if properly performed, is always
worth the cost"
THE PRIMARY ELECTIONS.
BY the results of the elections yesterday the ex
cellence of the new primary election law has
been fully attested. A closer scrutiny when
more time is given for a study of all the details of its
j operation may disclose minor defects here and there,
! but of the essential merit of the law there can be no
| longer any dispute. It has given honest citizens an
! opportunity to take part in the primaries with the as
| surance of having fair play, and they have to a large
tent profited by.it.
The victory and the triumph of the day is the sue
! cess of the better element of the Republican party in
i the contest against the bosses. That contest was, in
j fact, the only important issue of the elections. At
the Democratic primaries it was but a struggle of
.boss against boss; a case of dog eat dog. From the
' results of the Democratic primaries, therefore, no
. : valuable lesson can be drawn.
In the Republican primaries the contest was made
|in earnest for good government. The taxpayers, the
I property-owners, the industrious working-men of the
: party, responded to the appeal of The Call and took
; up the fight against the would-be bosses of the party
: j and achieved a signal and impressive victory. The
I party convention will be made up of representative
j men, to whom the people can look with reasonable
; expectation for a good ticket.
It is now clear that under the new primary law good
! citizens will be no longer at the mercy of a political
: boss, and if they surrender to him it will be their own
! fault. Republicans have shown they will not consent
; to have their party controlled by bosses having head
i quarters in saloons and relying for power upon ward
strikers of the lowest type. In .the Republican camp
: the saloon boss has been overthrown, and encourage
! ment has thus been given to the better class of Demo
j crats to make a similar fight for the purification of
'. their party in the next election.
For the present the political interest of the city
: centers in the Republican convention. It matters
, little whether the Buckley gang, that of Rainey and.
the Examiner or that of the Committee of One Hun
: dred win out in the Democratic fight. A strong Re-
I publican ticket, nominated by such men as were j
I elected yesterday, will command the votes not only of ]
all Republicans but of all independent citizens and of j
I a considerable number of Democrats who have suffi- j
, cient intelligence and civic patriotism to set the pub
j lie welfare above mere partisanship.
j Every indication of the day points to a triumph for
good citizenship in the coming election. The worth
iof the primary law has been vindicated. The business
! men and the workingmen who, in compliance with
! the urgings of The Call, left their private interests for
I a while to go to the polls have the satisfaction of
; knowing this morning that they have saved their party
; from the domination of saloon bosses and taken a
! long step toward assuring the inauguration of the
j municipal government under the new charter by a
! thoroughly representative Republican administration.
As the platform of the Democratic State conven
tion in Maryland says nothing at all about free silver,
; or gold standard, or anything else relating to the
j monetary problems of the country, it may be accepted
! as an evidence that Gorman is in the fight against
Bryan and is making it easy for all sorts of Demo
â¢ crats to stand with him without crowding one
I another.
There was a deficit in the revenues of the Treasury
in July amounting to more than $8,500,000, but as it
was the smallest July deficit we have had in five
years, there is nothing to fret about. A few years
more o( Republican administration and we will be
having another surplus for Democrats to howl over.
It appears Carnegie has been appointed a "Justice of
the Peace in Scotland, which shows what a difference
there is between that country and ours. We some
times make a millionaire out of a Justice of the Peace
in the United States, but we never make a Justice of
the Peace out of a millionaire.
LOST IDEALS.
A SHORT time ago Mr. Vardaman, a Demo
cratic leader of some repute in Mississippi, an
nounced his opposition to the education of the
negro and said:
"It is folly, and criminal folly, to talk about ad
vancing the negro race by education. The negro
is not permitted to advance, and his education simply
spoils a good field hand and makes a shyster lawyer
or fourth-rate teacher. It is simply money thrown
away. Booker Washington's school is no better than
the others. It would be all right in a nation of
negroes, but it is useless in the South. There is no
field of usefulness for its graduates. We might a3
well look the matter squarely in the face. It is .futile
to attempt to elevate the negro. The education of
the race would be a discouraging task under any con
ditions. Under those that exist and will continue to
exist in Mississippi it is simply preposterous."
Prior to the outbreak of imperialism and the new
craze for "killing niggers" in the Philippines for the
purpose of extending commerce, such a speech, com
ing from a responsible leader in the South, would
have been greeted by the Republican press with con
demnation short and sharp. The conscience of the
North would have been appealed to, and Republicans
would have been everywhere aroused to continue the
struggle' for enforcing throughout all parts of the
Union the law which gives to the colored man, as
well as to the white man, the right to an education
and to the freedom to enjoy it.
It has been noted by the New York Post, however,
that no such outburst has taken place now. In fact,
some stanch Republican papers, even in New Eng
land, speak rather commendingly of it. The Post
points to these comments as an evidence that Republi
can ideals have been lost, by such papers as have been
affected by the imperial craze, and they are now as
willing to make the negro a mere burden bearer in the
South as to reduce to subjection the natives of the
Philippines.
In summing up a review of the changed tone of so
many imperial organs, which still call themselves Re
publican, the Post says:
"We are maintaining absolute government in Cuba,
and trying to set it up in the Philippine Islands.
Whoever does not submit and obey is shot, just as ob
streperous negroes are shot in the Southern States.
Doubtless our fellow citizens of the South would
say that after the negroes had been taught to submit
and behave themselves they might be elevated. But
they demand entire submission first, and no imperial
member of the Republican part}', nor any militant ex
panisonist. can consistently denounce them. Our Gov
ernment has adopted the "shotgun policy" abroad, and
people that support it are estopped from condemning
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 9, 1899.
similar practices at' home. This result is exactly what
was predicted when we rushed into war, and it is to be
hoped that many who thought that war might be -made
a civilizing agency are learning from experience what
they would not learn from reason."
Vice-Chairman Stone of the Democratic National
Committee says that in 1900 Democracy will not fuse
with Populism or anything else, so it is clear Tom
Watson pointed a moral even if he did not adorn the
tale of Democracy in 1896.
THE FLIGHT CF THE KISSING BUG.
OUT of the infinite there descended upon the
United States early in the summer a dreadful,
horrible, invisible, monstrous thing, which
soon became known far and wide as "'the kissing
bug." A denizen of the night, black as a bat and as
noiseless as a ghost, the creature came and menaced
the land. So portentous was he, and so widespread
was the panic fear of him, the summer was robbed
of its best joys. No maiden would venture out alter
dark for a moonlight walk, and the boys. who were
caught out after the shades of night had been pulled
down stayed where they were, not daring to go home
until dawn.
The first stingful* kiss of the monster was reported
from New Jersey, but immediately thereafter he was
heard from all along the Atlantic coast, from Cape
May to Bar Harbor. From every salient point on that
long line the rumors started west, and soon reports of
the kissing were heard from every prairie, plain and
j mountain. First we would hear of a gentle girl hay
! ing the kissing spring of her sweet lip broken by a
j caress of the bug, then would come a tale of a young
i man getting his optics bunged out of sight by the
! creature. More fearful stories came later. Tragedies
! began to pour in, and for a time it was a dull day j
with the Associated Press when it did not have sev
-1 eral kissing bug fatalities to give a deep, dark tint to
the news of the day.
During the period when the creature raged almost
I every kind of injury done in the land was attributed !
ito him. Whenever an old soak "had 'em again" he j
j swore he had seen a kissing bug. When Fitzsimmons j
I awoke after the knockout blow, he asked his weeping ;
I seconds, "Was it Jeffries or a kissing bug?" When j
Bryan was silent for a week a rumor ran round the j
I land that the kissing bug had landed him one on the ,
jaw. r !
i For a time all sorts of creatures were suspected of \
j being the kissing bug. Entomologists were called upon
! to examine every kind of insect thing the wide repub-
I lie of the United States can furnish. In no instance,
however, was a specimen of the bug discovered. The
creature remained as invisible as he was dreadful.
', There were swollen lips, blackened eyes and distorted
noses in evidence all over the country, and there were
: veracious and reliable people who had seen the
1 corpses of persons slain by the bug, but the bug him
: self no one ever saw.
Suddenly, without warning, the bug came, and as
j suddenly he went. Mose Gunst never did the turn of
came and went more quickly. The bug was with us all
over the Union but a week or so ago, and now he is
nowhere. The boys and the girls have resumed their
moonlight rambles at the summer resorts, the dark
: ness no longer .has terrors for young or old. The
1 kissing.bug has gone.
During the thrill. of excitement which stirred the
hearts of the people just before the outbreak of the j
' war with Spain a certain, or perhaps it was an uncer- j
tain, yellow journal offered $100,000 reward for the j
men who blew up the Maine. The money has never
i been called for, and the sum is therefore available for
:an equally useful purpose. Let it be promptly offered
: for a kissing bug. j
Examiner reporters may breathe easy again. Now
that the City Receiving Hospital has been reopened
they will not have to submit to vaccination to swell
the list of fake cases in the yellow fellow's latest
costless charity.
A MOVEMENT has been started by the Manu
facturers' and Wholesalers' League to attract
trade to San Francisco through inducements
offered to merchants in the Pacific Coast States â
Idaho, Montana, Nevada, Utah, Wyoming, Arizona,
New Mexico â and British Columbia. Among the in
ducements to be offered are reduced transportation
rates to and from the city, and reduced charges at
hotels and theaters.
Such an undertaking is not a novelty in American
| commercial life. Some years ago Chicago offered
I special inducements to retailers in the Southern States
-. with the object of drawing that vast trade from East
| crn cities to Chicago. The venture proved so success-
I ful that New York was compelled to offer equal in
! ducements not to Southern merchants only but to
1 those of all parts of the country. Since then every
, large commercial center in the East, from Boston, to
I St. Louis, has adopted the plan, and it has now be
-1 come an established feature of commercial activity in
i the older parts of the Union.
The effect of the inducements offered by the rival
| cities of the East has been felt even at this distance,
and not a few Pacific Coast buyers have been moved
by them to make the long journey to the East to pur
; chase, goods that might have been obtained in this
i city upon equally advantageous terms. Such being
; the condition of affairs it is clear our wholesalers and
! manufacturers must exert themselves to hold the trade
i qf their natural market or it will be taken from them
| by their energetic competitors beyond the mountain**.
Of course an enterprise of this kind will have little
or no success unless carried out upon a large scale
and supported by merchants and manufacturers gen
erally. A considerable expenditure of money and
energy will be required to get the movement well
started, and if any large number of dealers show a de
sire to profit by the enterprise without sharing in the
work or in the expense, those engaged in promot
ing it will soon weary. For that reason there ought
to be a cordial co-operation of all dealers who are
concerned in the upbuilding of our wholesale trade.
The plan outlined by the committee in charge of
the undertaking has been well considered. and prom
ises to yield good results. It is one of the many new
movements in business circles which are breaking up
the past conservatism in trade, methods and starting
currents of increasing activity and energy. As the
only metropolitan city within thousands of miles, San
Francisco has naturally a strong attraction to all peo
ple on the Pacific Coast and if the league add to those
natural advantages the further inducements it is pro
posed to hold out to merchants and traders of the in
terior, the results can hardly fail to be of vast benefit
to. the whole community.
It is now evident good citizens will attend primary
elections when they are assured of fair play and an
honest count, and it is further evident that when they
do go to the polls victory is theirs. ,
There will be about as many objectors this morning
to the primary law as there are bosses and ward
striker*

ILLUSTRATIONSâ ReguIar Subscrib
er, City. The illustrations by Howard
Chandler Christie in one of the current
magazines are what are known as half
tone -wash drawings. They are made
With a soft pencil and the shade effects
produced by rubbing with the thumb. The
paper used is moderately smooth good
drawing paper. The tint of the picture
that is. where white effect is desiredâ
removed with a graver.
NEW ORLEANS CEMETERIESâ Em.,
City. As the soil of New Orleans is not
of sufficient density to permit of digging
down into it there are no graves in the
cemeteries, but the dead are all placed in
tombs above ground. Many of these are
beautiful and costly, but the most are
large vaults of masonry, consisting of
rows of cells, superimposed on each
other, generally to the height of seven or
eight feet. Each cell is just large enough
to admit of placing a coffin therein and
Is hermetically bricked up at its narrow
entrance as soon as the funeral service is
over. . .
INGERSOLLâ J. J. and Others, City.
In 1884, when the late Colonel Ingersoll
was in San Francisco, he delivered some
lectures. These were replied to in a pub
lic meeting by the reverend gentleman
named in the communications, and he
said: "Before I conclude I here publicly
challenge Colonel Ingersoll to refute any
one statement made by me here this
evening, and if he. cannot bring forward
any better or more convincing reason for
the non-existence of God than a mere
idea, let him. in all decency, retire -from
the profession of infidel teaching and
cease to mislead the unreflecting, the
shallow-minded, the brainless." That
was the -xtent of the challenge. Colonel
Ingersoll did not take any notice of It,
and there the matter ended. .- ,C "

When We Have the Best of Him.
From Life-.
THE newspapers tell of a curious mis
fortune that has overtaken Archie H.
Brower - of Bonesteel, S. D. Mr.
Brower was part owner of a tent
show, whereof sn important attraction
was an animal which the showman de
clared to be the missing link. They laid
stress on the inability of any observer to
determine whether the creature was a
monkey or a man. Unfortunately, this
missing link got fractious the other day.
and Brower, losing patience, hit it so hard
on the head with a club that it inconti
nently died. Now Brower is sure it was
only a monkey, but the authorities of
Bonesteel doubt his competence as an an
thropologist, and have bound him over
under charge of murder to await the judg
ment of the grand jury.
Uncle Sam and all of us are in a posi
tion to sympathize heartily with Mr.
Brower. We have had a missing link in
our show for nearly a year now, and for
months past he has been fractious, and
we have been pounding him on the head
in the effort to make him behave, squab
bling all the time among ourselves as to
his standing as a ' creature. We must
hope that our missing link, which has
more lives than Brower's, .will survive
the discussion; but, in any case, we
ought to be thankful that no grand jury
is likely to pass upon our actions.
The Examiner's Effrontery.
, Willows Journal.
The Examiner has assumed the role of
Prime Minister, and sends advice to the
President daily.
LATEST STORIES
of the
FUNNY MAN.
He Had a it.
The merchant uttered a sharp exclama
tion and sank back in his chair. A tele
gram fell from his shaking hand. His
eyes were wide, his face white, and beads
of perspiration stood on his brow. The
men in the outer offlce whispered among
themselves.
"Touch of heart disease?" asked one. â¢
"No; the old man \ can't make up a
note," said another. "I've heard rumors
of that kind. We fellows will have to be
looking for another place." â¢-.-â â ;
"(let to workâ he's coming to again."
The merchant wiped his brow, fetched a
despairing sigh, picked up the paper from
the floor, frowned and stamped his foot,
as if to summon all his resolution, placed
the telegram on his desk and forced him
self to read the bitter message. This
was it:
"Dearest James: Please send the waist
for my grosgrain suit at once. You will
remember the one, as it has revers on the
fromage and chain-stitched biases on the
back. It is in the lower trunk in the cup
board beside the back room, under your
winter overcoat. If not there it must be
In the sealed box on the third shelf in the
front room cupboard. If you don't And
it there it must be somewhere else. The
trunk keys are In second wardrobe draw
er, unless they were put in the chiffonier;
and I think the keys are in a vase on one
of the mantels. Pack the waist so as
not to wrinkle it; and, on! James, please
do not swear! Your loving wife."
Needed a Shave.
He had been out so late the night before
that he did not know at what hour he had
come home. When he awoke he was
curious to learn just how "rocky" he
looked. He accordingly reached out for
the silver-backed hand mirror that lay on
the table beside his bed. Instead of it he
got hold of the silver-backed hair brush.
Not recognizing his mistake he took the
brush up and gazed at the bristles for a
moment. Then he felt of the silver back
and then stared back at the bristles.

JURORS' SUIT IN COURT.
Appeal Will Be Taken From a Pro
Forma Order.
The suit of William Burch against the
Board of Supervisors, which was .recently
instituted to compel the auditing and
payment of the fees of jurors sitting in
the various criminal departments, was
taken up by Judge Seawell yesterday
morning. Attorney Louis P. Boardman
appeared for petitioner and City and
County Attorney Lane represented the
respondents.
In order to save time Mr. Boardman
consented that a pro forma order giving
judgment for the respondents be en
tered. From this order, on an .agreed
statement of facts, Mr. Boardman will
take an appeal to the Supreme Court,
and as the briefs are about completed
the higher court will soon decide this
important matter.
An Attorney Disbarred.
F. S. O'Donnell. an attorney in Stanis
laus County, several months ago.Â»ook a
prominent part in aiding a prisoner to
escape from the officers of the law. The
citizens did not take kindly to the trick
and the fellow was arrested, tried and sen
tenced to serve a term in the State prison
at Folsom. The matter was brought to
the attention of the Supreme Court, which
cited him to show cause why. he _hou!d
not be disbarred. Yesterday the court
considered the nature of the felony for
which O'Donnell was sentenced and en
tered an order disbarring him as an at
torney.

The regents of the University of Cali
fornia met yesterday afternoon, and
though much business was submitted but
little was definitely accomplished. The
absolute power with which the new presi
dent, Benjamin Ide Wheeler, is vested.
and his absence from the meeting of the
regents, necessarily delayed much Impor
tant business. In view of the fact that
the university is to reopen on the 16th of
this month it was ordered that the
"recommendations" be telegraphed Presi
dent Wheeler, and that he immediately
signify his decision in the matter.
A gratifying recommendation the re
gents were called to pass upon was the
following letter from Mrs. Hearst:
"W. A. Mi'Kowen Esq.â My Dear Sir: Replyin-?
to your letter of August 3 1 will write to say
that I stand ready to meet the expenses to be
incurred by the appointment of a woman phy
sician In the department of physical culture.
Since you ask to know my wishes in the mat
ter, I would say that if the appointment be
made I should be glad to see it awarded to
Dr. Mary Bennett Hitter of Berkeley. Yours
very sincerely, PHEBE A. HEAP.ST.
Fleasanton, Cal., August 7, I_>9.

ARIONS WILL SING.
Monster Concert on Saturday Even
ing in Honor of New York Club.
The Arions have completed arrange
ments for their monster concert on Satur
day evening in honor of the New York
Arions, the foremost singing society in the
United States, and who on that occasion
will be the guests of the local club. The
concert will take place at Odd Fellows'
Hall and an unusually fine programme of
vocal and instrumental selections will be
presented. ' â â¢' .' .
The entertainment committee is making
elaborate preparations for the affair,
which promises to be an immense Success.
The hall is to be handsomely decorated,
and the committee in charge will be re
splendent with green ribbon badges.

New Montgomery street. San Francisco.VOLUNTEERS' DAY TO
BE MADE A HOLIDAY
Everybody Favors This Plan of
Commemorating the Return
of the Boys,
important Matters to Be Settled at Today's Meetings,
The Headquarters Tent in Union Square
Dedicated.
.-__ ,___ HE suggestion that the Governor
of California proclaim a legal holi
day in honor of the return of th.
__ California boys from the Philip
pines has taken like vaccination.
It was made Monday night at the meet
ing of the Native Sons' and Native
Daughters' commit and yesterday
morning it whs on the tongue of every one
interested in the return of the volunteers.
"By all means,'' was the expression j
from every lip, "the Governor should
proclaim a holiday."
At the committee rooms of the Native j
Sons and Daughters in the California ,
Hotel, at the headquarters tent in Union
square Park, where the Red Cross ladies
will provide for the ease and comfort of
the soldier boys: a* the meeting places of
the various sub-committees of the citi
zens' reception committee, the same an
swer was given to the question.
"Favor it? Of course we do."
On the impulse of the moment many
gave it as their opinion that the holiday
should be that on which the boys set foot
again on their native shore. Others, who
took more time to determine, favored the
da*, following, for the very good reason
that by that time, after a good night's
si.ep. the boys would have had time to
get the cramps of shipboard out of their
limbs and be able to enjoy the good tilings
prepared for them. In addition, it was
urged: little could be â 'â â â â¢â  for the volun
teers on the day they landed other than
lunch them at the ferry, march them to
the Presidio in the afternoon and again
in the monster procession at night under
the- glow of thousands of incandescent
lamps.
On the day of the i ding, too, it is
urged few employers will insist on a full
day's work from their employes. Already
nuinv have announced their Intention of
making landing day a half holiday. Tins
will be sufficient to allow all to greet the
boys as they steam in. and then how
nicely will come next day with its legal
holiday aspect.
The holiday; of course, will be fixed by
the Governor, but he will certainly con
sult the wishes of the people who propose
it. These wishes will be put in official
form to-day at the conference committee,
and the result will be forwarded to the
Cwernor to reinforce the communication
ordered by the Native Sons ami Daugh
ters asking that he designate California
Volunteers' day.
Another thing that will take tip much
..ft:- attention 6f the conference com
mittee is the question of the time of the
big parade. It was the first intention of
the Native Sons and Daughters, who ini
tiated the movement for the reception,
that it should be held In the afternoon
a* Monday night's meeting they were ad
dressed by \V. J. Martin, W. P. Sullivan
Jr. and Henry McPlke of the citizens
committee, who sought to impress upon
them the advantages of a night parade
under the mass of electric lighting to be j
provided by the decoration committee.
The Native Sons and Daughters took
kindly to the suggestion and forthwith
appointed a committee to meet this morn
ing a like committee from the citizens'
organization. The plan was discussed yes
terday in an formal way. Grand Mar
shal Pistolesl and his assistants inter
viewed a largo umber of those represent
ing organizations that have signified a
willingness to parade and found a unani
mous sentiment in favor of the night
parade. In speaking last night of the
matter. Grand Marshal Pistoles! said:
"While the committee from the recep
tion committee appointed last night has
not yet had a formal meeting, yet the
individual sentiment of each member of
that committee is strongly in favor of the
night parade. It is almost Certain that
each member will go prepared to-morrow
morning to tell the executive committee
that the parade as suggested will be de
cided upon and that the Native Sons and
Daughters will bend all their ; energies to
make the affair a credit to both organiza
tions."
The primary election yesterday was not
allowed to disturb the even tenor of the
way of those interested in preparations
for the reception of the home-coming vol
unteers. Early in the morning dozens and
dozens of the collectors appointed by the
executive council of the nnanoe commit
tee started out books it. hand, to jot
down memoranda of descriptions to the
fund. On every hand they met wit 1 open
armed reception to judge from the flat
tering reports made at the sitting of the
council.
Everybody seems willing to contribute
something to the reception fund and be
fore the citizen collectors get through
everybody will have the opportunity. I*
is the programme now to have subscrip
tion agents in all the districts mapped out
not iter than this afternoon. By Satur
day night most of those who are to be
seen will have been seen.
The headquarters tent was the scene of
much activity during the day. Miss Jo
sephine Molloy. the newly appointed clerk,
was installed in her cosy quarters ready
to do a banking business; electric lights
ere being put in- and the interior be
ing decorated for the dedication in the
evening, which drew thousands of people.
The theatrical managers put in the day
adding the finishing touches to the pro
gramme for the big benefit and making
ready for the big social crush expected
at the Orpheum to-morrow at the auc
tion sale of boxes and choice seats, which
begins promptly at noon. No person who
pretends to be anybody can afford to miss
it. It will be a swell function and enter
tainment in itself that will rival the gen
eral run of theatrical performances and,
best of all, it costs nothing. The Mayor
and many of the beauties of the dramatic
profession will be on hand, a concert and
ait. merit will be given and Olograph
pictures of the soldiers in the trenches

which a grateful people stand ready to

ippines." Father Doherty said in part:

ourselves, and is a debt of gratitude for

ceive empty woids of welcome after hav
ing sacrificed everything. Let our citizens

; filers who went to the Sacramento riots

California. Let them not come back and

citizen-soldier becomes the soldier-citizen â¢

Father Doherty then paid a tribute to

: and making their return glorious.

ALL ALONG THE LINE
The Decoration Committee Will
Decide Its Plans at Once.
The decoration committee worked all

! for the electric display along Market
! street and have about decided on the plans
i suggested by Mr. Hirsch, which provides
I for rows of electric lights across Market
street from the Ferry, building to City
Hall avenue at a distance of about lilt*. .
feet apart, each Wire to contain no. less
than twenty-four colored incandescent
lamps. In addition to this, the lllumlna
tion of the City Hall dome and the dome
of the Ferry building. las suggested by
Mr Mills has been decided on. This, re
quires in all about 10,000 Incandescent

street and 2500 on each of the domes. me
committee finds that the decorations -
above outlined will about exhaust the
amount of money set apart for that pur

BLACKMAILING HIGHBINDERS.
Posters Circulated by a Gang . De
manding Money From Passen
gers to China.
A blackmailing proposition of a gang
of highbinders, twelve in number, was
brought to the attention of Chief Lees
yesterday and the Chinatown squad has
been instructed to arrest any of the gang
on sight.
Posters have within the past day or two
been sent to every house and store in
Chinatown and have also been posted up
on the walls declaring that each Chinese
who intends to leave for China must pay
the gang $â¢* 50. If the money is not paid
the relatives and friends of the Chinese
will be held responsible and threats are
made that the consequences will be seri
ous.
By order of the Chief the posters were
torn from the walls yesterday and an ex
ample will be made of the highbinders if
caught.

COMMERCIAL AND FINANCIAL NEWS
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Cheap grades of Coffee active and firm.
Wheat a fraction lower, dull and weak.
Barley nominal at previous quotations.
Oats in moderate demand, with light receipts.
Corn and Rye continue dull and unchanged.
Some changes in Seeds. Beans rule dull.
Hay shoves rather more firmness under lighter receipts.
Rolled Farley declined. Fran and Middlings unchanged.
Another radical advance in Grain Bags.
Sweet Potatoes lower. Other descriptions steady.
Onions weaker. Vegatables in abundant supply.
Futter firm at the advance. Eggs and Cheese steady.
Eastern Poultry coming in freely.
Bartlett Pears in heavy supply and cheaper.
Apricot season over. Peaches and Plums plentiful.
Pried Apricots firm. Peaches steady and Apples easy.
No talk of new Prunes or Raisins.
Provision market in good shape, but less brisk.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure distribution over the country
west of the Missouri River continues similar
to that at the beginning of the week. Condi
tions are favorable for thunderstorms in the
Rocky Mountain region, with cloudy and cool
weather over the greater portion of the Pacific
â *-Â°re-

gi eater portion if California. Nevada. I'tah
and Arizona. It is .-till about four decrees

A maximum wind velocity of 30 miles per
hour from the west is reported at Pocatello.

EASTERN MARKET& j
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK. Aug. B.â The stock market was
a dull affair for the greater part of to-day,
drifting aimlassly within a narrow range.
Some strength was shown by a few isolated
stocks throughout the day under the influence
which was not disclosed by any published news.
This persistent strength in some spots seemed
to arouse the apprehension of the bears in the
last hour of the market. The manner in
which prices were marked up in the late deal
ings indicated a good deal of a scramble by
the shorts to cover their contracts and awak
ened more appearance of animation and
strength than has been seen in the market for
some time. The higher prices for Americans
maue in London were without much effect and
they were for the most part nominal, and
London sold from 12,000 to 15,000 shares here
aft the opening. Strength kept cropping at
new points as the day progressed. There was
early strength in Anaconda and a few of the
specialties, but a period of reaction followed
under realizing in Tobacco and Kansas City,
Pittsburg and Gulf. Then the Minneapolis and
St. Louis stocks rose 5% and 3*4 respectively.
There was a late movement in the coalers
which lilted them from 1 to 2%, with a not
able effect on the whole list. ; Other stocks
showing notable gains were Consolidated Gas.
4**4. and Pacific Coast second preferred, 2%;
Tennessee Coal, Colorado Fuel. People's Gas.
Brooklyn Transit, Steel and Wire, Linseed OH
preferred and a few less prominent stocks ad
vanced a point. A very strong upward move
ment In Sugar from 159% to 162*4 was the de
cisive Influence in the late strength of the
market, which closed active and strong at net
gains in nearly all cases. The ton,, of the .
money market was called rather easy, though
rates were not quotahly changed. Sterling ex
change here continued Its downward course and
confidence grew that gold will come from
abroad if an emergency here demands It.
The free offerings of funds from outside
sources In the New York money market In
creases the belle" that small demands will be
made upon New York fur money to move the
crops. There were late reports of weakness In
the London market for silver on account of
importations from Chile.
Kansas City, Pittsburg and Gulf first re
acted In sympathy with the stock. Otherwise
changes were small and mixed. Total sales,
par value $1,320,000.
United States bonds unchanged on bid quota
tion.

I CHICAGO, August The opening prices for
September Wheat showed a decline of %@%c
from yesterday, September starting at 6S%@
68% c, and that proved to be the highest point
of the da>*. Interest In the market, which has
j been slack for several days, was, if possible, ;
even duller than yesterday, and the market
for th? greater part of the session kept within
a range of %c. The weakness at the start was
due to a decline at Liverpool of %d since Fri- j
day. This was double the decline in the local
market since that day and coupled with the j
, continued absence of foreign inquiry for Amer- ;
lean Wheat kept prices on the down grade
during most of the day. It was apparently I
the prevailing opinion that it was a hopeless
task to attempt to bull the market while there
is so little demand for Wheat abroad, ami
what little buying was done was against puts,
ito which price â 68%_68%c-fthe market de- i
clined shortly after the opening. There was
scattered selling by the northwest and by local
commission houses, but outside interest was
slack. September longs were Inclined to trans
fer their deals from that month to Decem
ber and buying of the latter against sales of
j September had a tendency to widen the spread
! between these months to the advantage of the
J carriers. September got down to 68% c. A
: slight!} better feeling developed near the close,
I shorts being fair buyers at the bottom and
j the market rallied slightly, closing at 68%Q
I 6S%c.
i Corn was fully as dull as Wheat, but much
! steadier. The market was weak at the start,
! but the strong cash situation sustained prices.
September closed a shade higher.
Oats were dull and irregular. The cash de
| mand was excellent and receipts were large.
' September closed a shade lower. May- closed
with an advance of %c.
Provisions were heavy throughout the ses
sion. Packers were free sellers, apparently on
i the belief In a freer hog movement and sub-
I stantial support was lacking. The market
: tended steadily downward and was decidedly
weak at the close. September Pork lost 12.c,
Lard 10c and Ribs 7%C

! New York Dried Fruit Market, j
! d â  . I
._> . â <-Â»
The New York Commercial says: "Prunes
are quiet and spot trading Is not large. The
! carry-over promises to be larger than holders
i wish, hut still if the present rate of distribu
! tion continues there will be comparatively few
I In this market. On the coast some 300 cars
may be left, though probably this is an outside
estimate and may not be borne out by facts.
The growing crop appears to be in good con
dition so far as California is concerned, and
the output promises to be large. France re
ports not over 30,000,000 pounds, compared with |
90,000,000 two years ago. Oregon will not send
out over 300 cars, as against an anticipated
output of 1000 cars. The market should, there
fore, prove favorable for high prices on Cali
fornlas, and holders are looking forward to
brisk trade. --Â-s*.'':
"Peaches are quiet and held firmly up to
quotations. There are rumors of sales of sev
eral cars for forward delivery at private prices,
but no confirmation of the report is obtain
able. New crop will not be along until next
month or toward the last of this. Quotations
are around 6%c for future delivery, and holders
show no disposition to shade the figures.
"Apricots continue steady, with sales at
9@9%a Buyers appear to be certain that a
decline ' will follow the present strong situa- i
tion, but holders are apparently equally cer
tain that quotations will hold stiff at present
rates. There is little business as a result of
the divergence of views, and no one can tell
when the deadlock will lie broken.
"Raisins attract some more attention than
they did last week at this time, but sales -are
! now rather more quiet. Seedlings and ungraded
I varieties are in most demand, and are held
j firm. The outlook for business is encouraging.
i and good sales are expected. Reports concern-
I ing damage to the growing crop are vague,
and as yet do not exert much influence. If
there is any loss well informed dealers here
say that it will be largely on first crop. The
second crop will probably be safe and quite
I up to expectations."

General Merchandise.
BAGSâ Bags have again advanced
i sharply. Calcutta Grain Bags, 6*_c; Wool
Bags, 264.28 c. .
Wellington. $8 per ton; New Welling
ton, $8; Southfleld Wellington, $7 50; Seattle.
$6; Bryant, $6; Coos Bay, $5; Wallsend, $7 50;
Scotch, $8; Cumberland, $9 In bulk and $10 50
in sacks; Pennsylvania Anthracite Egg, $12;
Cannel, $8 per ton; Rock Springs and Castle
Gate, $7 50; Coke, $12 per ton In bulk and $14
ln sacks.
Since the Mariposa left, the following Aus
tralian coal cargoes were delivered here, viz..
Earl of Dunmore, 3575 tons; Carrollton, 2131
tons; Invercoe, 2347 tons; total. 8253 tons. The
total arrivals here of Colonial coal since June 1
up to date only foot up 27.278 tons, whereas In
the same period last year the arrivals amounted
to 60,279 tons. This is very singular, as our crops
J this year were declared better than the average
" five months ago, which would naturally have
headed tonnage in this direction, whereas m
IS9B at the same time our harvest was a pro
nounced failure. This clearly demonstrates
that there is an unprecedented demand for ton
nage everywhere, and we are now feeling Its
effects locally. The total coal tonnage listed
for here from Newcastle and Sydney foots up
about 72,000 tons; last year in August it was
over 90,000 tons. Last freight quotations from
Newcastle are quoted at 16s to 16s 6d per ton
for prompt loading.
Coal quotations are established now entirely
by the Coast Colliery Agents, as the small
quantity of foreign coming to hand cuts a very
small figure in making prices. No material ad
vance has been made, but the market Is steady
and later on better prices may be looked for,
as coast tonnage is so scarce it will be a task
to keep our local requirements fully supplied.
There will shortly be some steam colliers added
to our carrying fleet.

NOTEâ the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
â given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

YOUNG WIFE
FINDS SHE IS
NUMBER TWO
Husband Has Eloped
With a Third.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
SOS Broadway, Aug. 8.
A dainty little woman not over 20 years
if age told a story in the District Attor
ney's office this afternoon that uncovers
remarkable duplicity in a man not much
older than herself.
Mrs. Rosie Wolf was married in Sacra
mento just a year ago. and now she has
made the discovery that her husband is a
bigamist and that she has no legal claim
to the name of the man who is now living
with a third young lady at Tcsia and go
ing hy the name of Charles Eeiter.
Some time after her marriage to Wolf
Mrs. Rosie Wolf detected certain discrep
ancies in her husband's account of his
past thai led her to believe that another
Mrs. Wolf was living somewhere in the
Kast. She employed a detective, to whom
she confided her suspicions, and a few
weeks ago she received a letter from the
first Mrs. Wolf, stating that Wolf had
deserted her and three children some time
ago and had also taken with him the pro
ceeds of a house and lot which, he had
sold and which was their joint property.
Immediately on receiving this Informa
tion young Mrs. Wolf, who was then re
siding at Emeryville, told him of her dis
covery and at once left his home, return
ing to her parents in San Francisco. Wolf
made no attempt to deny the existence of

his first wife nd family, but sought to ,
effect a compromise with his second wife j
â which would prevent him from having to j
meet the consequences of committing big
amy. Several meetings were held between j

at the oflice of Attorney Mitchell, ann
Wolf agreed to contribute $12 a month to
the support ot his second wife and gave
her an order on the Judson Iron Works,
â¢whore he had a good position, to draw her

As the end of the first month ap- -

loft his position and had drawn his pay .
up to the time of leaving.

lost sight of for several days. He had j
"disappear. . About ti e same time it j

Tvrer 19 years old. had also mysteriously
disappeared from her home in Golden
Gate. Mrs. Tvrer left a brief note with

she ha secured a good position but cou d
hot them until later .... she would

a divorce from his wife in Oakland.
When Mrs. Wolf No. 2 read or the

alty of the law and that it was evidently

astray under the pretext of marriage.

Alameda County News
JAKEY WAS
TOO FREE IN
HIS SPEECH
His Confession May
Be Damaging.
Oakland Oflice San Francisco Call,
' 90S Broadway. Aug 8.
The prize-fighters and the lovers of
sport who sacrificed a quarter apiece to
witness the recent basement mill ap
peared before Judge Smith this morning
with a small army of attorneys. After
a little conference It was agreed to have
sill the spectators represented by Attor
neys Colonel Garrity and Lin church.
Dr. Leroy Herrick and Will Tye were
called, but were not present, and Colonel
Garrity; for the missing defendants, said
they would be ready to explain their
presence in the basement to-morrow
morning, and a continuance was granted.
In addition to these cases Joe Rosenberg,
.1. S. Richardson, Fred Gatter, J. D. Eel
kins. Will Holland, Phil Simon and James
Galligan also had their cases passed till
to-morrow, when they will -.had.
George Duddy pleaded not guilty, and
said he wanted to be tried by the court
when he was a little less busy, and he
will be accommodated next Monday. Tom
Brady also wanted a separate trial and
will have it next Tuesday.
The situation is a little mixed. Green
and Permien, two young dad.- who acted
as bottle-holders, have pleaded guilty,
and will be sentenced to-morrow. Their
pleas will naturally affect the cases
against the spectators, who will hard
be able to prove that they were at the
ringside of the prize-fight and were not
looking on. Jakey Baumgarten, one of
the principals, made such a full con
fession that 'hi. Hodgkins is confident
of securing convictions. Jake was taken
from his bed at a quarter to 3 in the
morning. and shivering and scan he
went before Captain Peterson, who read
to him that section of the Penal Code re
lating to prize-fighting and Its penalties.
Jake was duly informed that anything
he might say might possibly be used
against him nt his trial, but between his
tears he told all hi knew, and thereby
lost many of his friends. Jake's con
fession is as full as though he realized
that by giving it he could prevent his
going to San Quentin for the five yearÂ«
which the law fixes as a maximum pen
alty for just such cases. With Jake con
fessing he was actually fighting for a
prize and without a permit, and with the
bottle-holders having pleaded guilty, the
alleged spectators will have to prove that
they were somewhere else at the time, or
else put up a novel defense.
Many are denying that they were pres
ent. Tin- arresting of the spectators is
still going on. and In addition to the
twelve landed yesterday William Hugh,
H. E. Alden. a real estate man, and
Charles Bock, a newspaper man. were
gathered In to-day, and many others have
promised to surrender themselves as soon
as they can procure the necessary $J0
with which to furnish bail. It now looks
as though that miserable fight in the
basement will net the city treasury about
$."iOO. while the value of the stake was
$9 20.
Libel Case Seeking a Judge.
OAF-LAND, Aug. Apparently neither
Judges Greene. Ogden nor Ellsworth is
particularly willing to try the $100,000 libel
suit brought by W. J. Dingee against Mrs.
Pedar Sather. The case came up origin
ally before Judge Ogden and he trans
ferred it to Judge Ellsworth's department,
but the latter transferred it back to the
former, who then sent it to Judge
Greene's department, and Judge Greene
yesterday transferred it back to Judge
Ellsworth. To-day Judge Ellsworth
promptly sent the case back to Judge
Greene. The Judges seem adverse to
handling the case,

AN ODD CLUBHOUSE
FOR BERKELEY WOMEN
0 i
\ T~"\ ERKELEY, Aug. B.â Within a few weeks the finishing touches will be T
T Jl put upon a new woman's clubhouse now going up on the corner of .
+ r~" l 'wight way and Dana street, which is attracting much interest and Q
x II curiosity on account of its peculiar oddity and eccentricity of design. <f
V â * * The building, though partially complete, already shows its main points -f
â¦ of outline, and these Indicate a structure of the most unlo.ue kind, a radical v
â¼ departure from the conventional, bordering almost upon, the freakish. The A
â¦ house Is being erected by the Berkeley Town and Gown Club, composed of I
A prominent society ladies in the university town, and is intended to be used 1.
V for the meetings, lectures, art exhibitions and receptions which the club T
