NETTIE SPEARS
TURNS TABLES
ON HER SPOUSE
He Sued Her for a
Divorce.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Aug. 8.
Morgan C. Spears, who only a few days
ago filed a sensational divorce suit in the
Superior Court of Alameda County against
bis wife. Nettie J. Spears, picturing her in
his allegations as an amazo'nlan who on
one occasion ordered lam at the point of
a revolver to quit the family home at
Sanger, Fresno County, lias had the tables
turned on him. He has been arrested on
a warrant issued in Sanger charging him
with failure to provide for his rive minor
children.
Spears Is a motorrnan in the employ of
the Oakland Transit Company, and Dep
uty Constable Irvie, who came all the
way from Sanger to effect the arrest, had
little difficulty In finding the man in blue
uniform and brass buttons. Spears was
decidedly surprised when placed under ar
rest, but through the pro: 1 1 > t action of
his attorneys. Goodcell & Mendenhall. who
rustled up bondsmen, he was later in the
day released by Superior Judge Ogden on
$600 bonds. Mr. and Mrs. Ben Wright go
ing his sponsors. He was held to answer
before the magistrate In Fresno County.
Though Spears, in his divorce complaint
made many charges of extreme cruelty,
explaining that he had left his wife rear
ing that his life would be endangered if lie
returned, Mrs. Spear, on the other hand,
will have a sad story of non-support to
relate when the case comes on for trial.
Recently she had the Society for the Pre
vention of Cruelty to Children intercede
for her and go on her deserting spouse's
trail.
Another interesting divorce suit was
filed to-day by Alexander Anderson, an
engineer by occupation, who in his com
plaint, prepared by Attorney H. S. Aid
rich alleges that his wife, Lizzie Ander
son, has deserted him. He not only
charges that the wife deserted ins bed and
board, but that she took the bed and most
of the other household furniture along
with her when she left. The parties were
married about a year ago and this is not
Anderson's first experience In divorce pro
ceedings. He was also divorced from his
two former wives, both Of whom. It is
said left him under circumstances very
similar to those Involved In the present
case. __
Ben J. V. Merle, a motorrnan on the
Alameda Electric line, . also commenced
proceedings to-day for a divorce from
Florence R. Merle*. Me. too. alleges that
his wife left him without provocation.
MUST CARRY LIGHTS.
Baby Carriages Not Exempt From
the Ordinance.
ALAMEDA, Aug. B.â The Board of City
i Trustees adopted an ordinance last night.
! under the provisions of which it will be
j necessary for any person propelling a
j baby carriage after nightfall to carry a
â¢ light. The ordinance is general in its
! scope and includes automobiles, hay wag-
I ons. bicycles, tricycles or vehicles of any
! kind. The ordinance as adopted is as
| follows:
1 Section 1. No person shall use or ride
upon any conveyance, bicycle, tricycle
or other vehicle on any public street
f or way in the city of Alameda after 8
o'clock in the evening or before sun
rise of any day between April 1 and
October 1 of any year, or after 6
o'clock in the evening and before sun
rise of any day betwen October 1 and
April 1 of any year, unless such con
veyance, bicycle, tricycle or other ve
hicle is provided with a light of at
hast one-candle power, projecting
rays of light In the direction In which
said vehicle is moving.
Sec. 2. Any person who shall violate
any of the provisions of this ordinance
shall be deemed guilty of a misde
i meanor, and upon conviction thereof
shall be punished by a fine not exceed
ing $20. or by imprisonment in the
! City Jail of th.- city of Alameda, Cal.,
for a term not exceeding ten days, or
by both such fine and imprisonment.
Sec. 3. Ordinance 212 is hereby re
pealed.
Sec. 4. This ordinance shall be pub
lished In the official paper of this
, city and take effect immediately there
after.
The above ordinance is to take the place
of one making it a misdemeanor for any
j person to ride a bicycle, etc., on the
| streets of Alameda after dark.
I The attention of the board was called by
1 the City Attorney to the unjustness of
i the ordinance to bicyclists, who were re
j quired to carry lights, while other ye
! hides were exempt, whereupon the board
I instructed him to prepare an ordinance to
i include all vehicles.
The merchants are displeased at the
board for passing such an ordinance.

THE TWIN HEROES
OF TWIN LAKES
OAKLAND, Aug. S. âH. .T. and A. R. Waters of Nil*B, proprietors of the
Herald, are heroes. They have made a record as life-savers at Twin
Lakes that will never be' forgotten so long as Baptist conventions are
held in the little resort near Santa Cruz.
Early last Sunday morning a party of young ladles and gentlemen,
consisting of Miss May Vernon and Miss Florence Page of East Oakland, G.
R. and W. C. Pruett of Oakland and A. It. and 11. G. Waters of Niles, left
Twin Lakes for the beach, having an engagement to breakfast at the Hotel
Capitola. They were Inspecting a cove and were hemmed in by a forty-foot
precipice. They were of the opinion that the tide was going out, but instead
it was coming in. After walking far into the cove they discovered their mis
take. There was nothing but steep precipices ahead of them, and their only
chance of .-scape was by the way they had gone In. The water was rapidly
deepening, and to get out they would have to brave the surf.
To save the young ladies they must be carried on the shoulders of the
gentlemen. This was a comparatively easy matter around the first two
points. The Waters brothers, being the larger of the men. bore the young la
dies, the Pruett brothers leading the way. Rut when the third point was
reached the surf was high and getting dangerously rough, and here was the
test of valor.
H. J. "Waters, the Pruett brothers leading and guiding him slowly through
the water, carried Miss Vernon around the point tirst. He then returned to
assist his brother and carried Miss Page out, A. U. Waters guiding. The task
was a severe one physically, but was accomplished with no dreaded results.
The last point was easily made, each of the Waters brothers carrying a
young lady through the surf, which was here shallow. When the party turned
about from their point of safety and looked hack at the beach just traveled
they saw that the coves were full and that the surf was angrily breaking over
tin- points. Cheers reached their ears from a bevy of anxious people on the
wharf as they came into plain sight, for they had been watched in their peril
and some fishermen asked to go to their rescue.
Word was sent to the relatives of the young ladles that they were safe,
for it was feared that th.- story of their danger would precede their return.

TRIAL OF IMPORTANT
SUIT IS COMMENCED
LARSEN SEEKS DAMAGES FOR
WRECKED HOME.
Awful Details of the Melrose Ex
plosion Will Be Recited Again
in Court.
_
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
9 8 Broadway, Aug. 8.
An important trial was commenced in
Judge Ogden's court this afternoon. The
case is that of Rasmus Larsen, who is
suing the Western Fuse and Explosives
Company for $1500 on account of the
wrecking of his home at Melrose by the
explosion which coat the lives of six
brave officers a year ago, and for the
next few days the awful details ol the j
explosion will be recited in court once j
more. : - â' ... .. ,
The Larsen suit is the first of the kind
to reach trial, and upon its ultimate out- j
come will depend the action to be taken
by many other property owners who sui- j
fered losses. Should Larsen win and the
company be held responsible, any num
ber of suits will be promptly filed. Th-;
particular point at issue is whether or
not the powder magazines maintained at
Melrose, and which were set off by a
murderous Chinese who was trying to
avoid capture, were really nuisances.
The greater part of this afternoon was
occupied in securing a jury. The ilaintitf
Is represented by Attorneys M. C. > hap
man and Reed & Nusbaumer, while
George R. Lukens is watching the com
pany- Interests.

in a gorgeous chariot, which is being
built for the occasion. Then will follow
the numerous cakewalkers in their differ
ent divisions ranging from the graceful
and dignified cakewalk to the grotesque
style of walking. There will also be two j
handsomely decorated floats representing

"Cakewalk Up to Date."

but More Is Wanted.
WASHINGTON, Aug. B.â The Assistant

chairman of the National Dewey Home
Fund Committee, said to-day in regard to

"The committee is very much encour
aged with the returns which are being
made. The total receipts now exceed $20,
--000, and even if no larger sum is realized
It is the purpose of the committee to pur
chase a home with that amount, it is
expected, however, that the fund will
grow considerably owing to the fact that
the admiral has not raised any objections
to receiving the gift. Many people who
were anxious to contribute held aloof
from the project owing to the circulation
of rumors that the admiral would not ac
cept the home extended him. But it .s
expected that the removal of this obsta
cle will induce many to contribute who
were stopped by the circulation of false
reports The whole business will be closed
before the arrival of Admiral Dewey, It
is now expected that he will arrive Sep
tember 1 instead of October 1. During
the next month 1 hope contributions will
come in rapidly and liberally. There
ought not to be near so much difficulty In
raising $40,000 or (50.000 throughout the
whole country when for the purpose of
giving the admiral a reception in New
York three or four times that amount has
been received and expended to make the
celebration a very prominent one.
"The home fund movement ls a project
which every section of the country should
have at heart. The hon is to be located
at the national capital. While this is a
long w-v from the Pacific States, yet they
are Indebted to the hero of Manila per
haps a little more, because of the fact
that they are nearer to the scene of his
glorious victory.
"It is highly probable that the commit
tee will decide to buy a house in the city
and remodel it. as no plans have been
called for as yet. The cause is a good one
Berkeley, Fruit vale
electric cakewa'k, which was given, in
San Francisco and which made a decided
hit there, Besides Miss Pearl Hickman;
Miss La Salle and Mr. Barr, who were
the participants In the walk across the
bay, there will be two juvenile walkers,
who will be elaborately gowned in black
and yellow satin cosutmes which are
being made expressly for the occasion.

left no longer in doubt, for she broke into
! tears and plteously pleaded for mercy.
! When sin- unbuttoned her coat tidies,

lawn dresses fell to th.- floor to corrobor

and In all the stolen property amounted

the owner's name had been effaced. There

Death of a Pioneer Resident.
ALAMEDA. Aug. B.â William Holts, one
of the oldest and best known citizens of
the West End, died early this morning at
the Alameda Sanitarium. He recently suf
fered a stroke of paralysis, from the ef
fects of which he never recovered.
Deceased was born in Hamburg sixty
nine years ago. He came to California in
181!* and engaged in the grocery business
in San Francisco. In 1869 he located In
Alameda and opened a grocery .--tore on
the corner of Santa Clara avenue ;nd
what is now known as Linden street. A
few years later he embarked in the read
estate and insurance business, which vo
cation he followed up to the time of his
last illness.
Mr. Holtz was twice elected a Justice
of the Peace He was an old fireman, be
ing a charter member of Whidden Hose
Company No. 2, and was at one time a
member" of the Board of Education. __c
was f* member of Oak Grove Lodge. F.
and A. M.. under whose auspices the fu
neral will be conducted.
Deceased leaves a widow and an only
daughter. Mrs. Johanna Jurgens. who is
now visiting In Hamburg with the family
of her father's brother. Fred Holtz, the
only son, died In 1889.

COLONIST RATES FIXED.
Transcontinental Roads Agree Upon
a Schedule.
CHICAGO, Aug. B.â All the Western
and transcontinental roads were repre
sented at the mass meeting at. the Great
Northern to-day to consider the proposi
tion to advance colonist rates to Califor
nia points regardless of the low rates in
effect to North Pacific Coast points. The
main obstacle in the way of a restoration
of the California rates was the fear that
the low North Pacific Coast rates might
be used as basing rates and thereby cut
California rates via North Pacific Coast
points
This obstacle was removed by the prom
ise of Passenger Agent Whitney of the
Great Northern that the low colonist
rates from St. Paul to North Pacific
Coast points would be limited strictly to
those points and would not be permitted
to be used as a basing rate. It was then
agreed to restore the California colonist
rates on September 1 to â  the old basis
of $52 60.

LIVERMORE IS
OUT FOR MEN
IN UNIFORMS
Wants a Company of
Guardsmen.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
808 Broadway, Aug. S.
Livermore is ambitious to furnish a per
manent company of the National Guard
of California. Many months ago a peti
tion was filed with the State authorities
asking that of the new companies to be
formed one be located In Livermore. The
petition was signed by fully twice the
number of eligible young men necessary
to form a company. , It is understood that
shortly the Board of Location will decide
where the new companies shall be placed.
1 There are three vacancies, one of which
was made by the dropping out of the Red
ding company, and the other two are in
the southern part of the State.
The Board of Location has the Liver
more petition In hand, and the only other
competitor for the honor, so far as known,
is Watsonville. The towns are using all
the influence they can command to gain
favorable action by the Board of Loca
tion, each town realizing that the adver
tisement that must follow from the forma
tion of a company is of Inestimable value.
The movement at Livermore was orig
inated by Dr. Summers, and his efforts
have been seconded by those of Assembly
men W. McDonald and J. O. McKown. It
is now propose. to Inaugurate a move
ment embracing the whole town, and to
try to bring the matter to a head at once.
The State is very generous in its allow
ances to National Guard companies, and
furnishes $1200 a year for the rent of an
armory, in addition to other money for
Incidentals. The only thing exacted of the
community Anally selected by the Board
of Location is that it shall be able to sus
tain a company. On this point it will be
an easy matter for Livermore to satisfy
the board, as there are at least three
times the necessary number of eligible
recruits in the town.

NOTICE of meeting of stockholders of The Ris-
don Iron, and !->,-.>::;. ,t ire Works, to take Into
consideration proposed Increase of capital
stock.
Notice is hereby given that pursuant to a
resolution and order of the board o* rectors
of The Risdon Iron and Locomotive Works, a
corporation organized and existing under and
by virtue of the laws of the State of Califor-
nia, and having its office and principal place
of business at the city and county of San
Francisco, in said State, unanimously adopted
at a regular meeting of said board held on j
the seventh day of August. in the year one
thousand eight hundred and nlnety-hlne, a
meeting of the stockholders of said corpora-
tion will be held on Wednesday, the Eight-
eenth Day of October, in the year one thou-
sand eight hundred and ninety-nine, at the
hour of two o'clock ln the afternoon of said
day, at the office of said corporation, which
is at the building situate on the easterly cor-
ner of Beale and Howard streets, in said city
and county of San Franclaeo, the same being
the principal place of business of said corpo-
ration, and being the building where the
beard of directors of said corporation usually
meets;
That the object of said meeting is to deter-
mine whither or not the capital stock of said
(ration shall be Increased from one million
. dollars, divided into ten thousand shares of
one hundred dollars each, to a capita! stock
of one million one hundred thousand dollars,
divided Into eleven thousand shares of one
hundred dollars each;
That the amount to which It ls proposed to
increase the paid capital stock is one million
one hundred thousand dollars.
By order of the board of director**.
L. P.. MEAD,
Beeâ 'taâ r of The Risdon Iron and locomotive
Works.

DOWNFALL OF THE POLITICAL BOSS
CAUSED BY THE NEW PRIMARY LAW
Republican Campaign to
Be Guided by Best
Element.
Complete Routing of the Self-
Constituted Party
Managers.
C=r- __*, IIIRTY-THRF.E THOUSAND
votes were cast at the primary
election yesterday and the peo-
I pie achieved a triumph over
*-*-" the political bosses. The over
whelming defeat of the regular organi
zation of the Republican party and the
sweeping victory won by the Central
Republican League signify that the
Republicans of San Francisco will not
take orders from saloon bossesâ they
â will not go i" the barrooms for political
guidance and inspiration.
The Republican bosses, Kelly and
Crimmins, sot what they deserved. At
Sacramento last winter, when the
manly, honest and fearless Republi
cans were making the greatest fight in
the history of California to preserve
the honor of the State in the United
States .-"rial.'. Kelly ami Crimmins
cast what little influence they pos
sessed to aid Burns and the railroad.
They have been relegated to political
obscurity where they may keep com
pany with the Mexican. Thedeath knell
of Bosses Crimmins and Kelly was
sounded when The Call defeated the
blanket ticket scheme.
At the headquarters of the Kelly-
Crimmins forces defeat was acknowl
edged early in the evening. An atmos
phere of gloom and lonesomeness settled
over the place and hardly a visitor called
to share the sorrow of the few faith
ful lieutenants who remained on guard
and hoped against hope that the bad
news that had been coming in over the
telephone since the polls closed might
be without foundation and that in the
end the lightning would strike them.
They admitted that they had been de
lea led, howeverâ hopeless, defeated.
They did not know in what districts the
punishment had been most severeâ
that did not seem to worry them. It
was sufficient for them to know that
they were no longer "'in it."
In marked contrast was the situation
at the Grand Hotel rendezvous. The
luums were crowded, figures were dis
played on the walls and congratula
tions were in order. As â¢ early as 9
o'clock the Central Republicans had re
turns showing that they had won out
in the Thirty-fourth, Thirty-fifth,
Thirty-sixth, Thirty-ninth, Fortieth
and Forty-second districts, while the
Crimmins forces conceded them
the Thirty-seventh and Forty-first.
The only unsatisfactory news was that
Kellv and Crimmins had carried the
Twenty-ninth district by the narrow
margin of three votes.
"A glorious victory for honest poli
tics" was the cry of the winners. They
gave all the credit to the decent ele
ment of the voters of the city and
claimed none for 'themselves.
The result of the election is looked
upon as a blessing to the real Repub
lican party. It means that the County
Committee, which will in future guide
the destinies of the organization, will
have a higher and more laudable object
than controlling the spoils of office. If
the convention is equal to the occasion
and nominates representative men
there is little question but that the vic
tory at the next municipal election will
be as sweeping as the uprising of yes
terday against bossism.
The reform Democrats, under the
leadership of Gavin McNab and Jasper
Donald, have won a victory over
the boss forces which were marshaled
by Buckley, Rainev and the Examiner.
Boss Buckley made a desperate fight
to gain control of the convention, but

snr^HE new primary law has proven the undoing of the political boss and the safeguard of.
7-MIE new primary laze has proven the undoing of the political boss and the safeguard of
decent citizens.
/ The defeat of the blanket ticket proposition by The Call was the beginning of the end of
boss rule, and the working of the primary law was the climax.
Reputable members of the Republican party had an opportunity yesterday to express their
will without- fear of having that expression nullified by the machinations of the bosses who make
their headquarters in saloons, and the result is that the convention will be in the hands of the best
clement of the party and the ticket which will be put forth by the Republican party will not bear the
taint of either corruption or bossism, but will be made up entirely of men selected by those who have
the best interests of the city at heart, and will be carried forward to overwhelming victory 'at the
polls.
Not only will the ticket be a clean one, but the men who will have charge of the party affairs for
the coming year will be selected by the same convention, and the people of San Francisco will be
treated to a campaign conducted on clean lines with but one end in view â good of the whole
city. Boss domination in the party has been relegated to oblivion, and the tax-eaters must now give
place to officials who will administer the city's affairs in a business way.
In the Democratic ranks it was a triangular fight between bosses and the Committee of One
Hundredâ self -constituted manager of affairs Democraticâ out against Boss Buckley on
one corner and the Rainey-Examiner boss on the other, leaving so much of bitterness that factional
lights will mar the serenity of local Democracy and help reputable citizens to gain the ascendency in
the management of the city's business through the clean ticket which -will be presented by the Repub
licans.
For these benefits the new primary law is accountable, and its present trial has shown con
clusively that the honest citizen â the reputable citizen, the decent citizen repudiated in most un
mistakable manner the domination of the "boss" in politics.
was defeated at every point. The
Rainey-Examiner league fought with
equal desperation to down the Commit
tee of One Hundred, but the political
sagacity displayed by Gavin McNab
was too much for the fat boss and his
newspaper ally.
In the Twenty-eighth Assembly dis
trict the Rainey and Committee of One
Hundred ticket, headed by J. D. Max
well, is elected: The independent ticket,
headed by Frank Sweeney, and the
Buckley ticket, headed by David
Bridgewood, are defeated.
The fight in the Twenty-ninth Dis
trict was close. Early returns gave
the victory to the combined forces of
Rainey and Buckley, but at a late
hour the claim was put forward, based
on the tally of the ballots, that the
Braunhart ticket, backed by the Com
mittee of One Hundred, would pull
through. Braunhart up to midnight
admitted defeat, but after that hour
said there was some slight hope of his
success.
One of the most interesting and ex
citing battles of the day was carried
on in the Thirtieth District between
the Buckley forces headed by Alec
Greggains and the Rainey-Phelan
combination under the leadership of
Assistant Chief Dougherty. The Thir
tieth was almost conceded to Greg
gains during the early part of the day,
but Dougherty's men made a bitter
battle and finally won the day. The
Dougherty-Greggains political feud
has become a historical event in the
Thirtieth. Years ago they did politics
together, but when Greggains left the
Fire Department he organized his
forces and opposed Dougherty in sev
eral primaries. Dougherty's sweeping
victory possibly ends the bitter fac
tional warfare which has existed in
the district for many years.
In the Thirty-first District a bitter
battle was waged between the inde
pendent forces of Tim Treacy and the

"END" WAS DEFEATED

Committee of One Hundred's ticket, j
headed by J. N. Renault. At midnight i
both factions claimed the victory. The |
Renault ticket placed its majority at j
eighty votes.
In the Thirty-second District the
Committee of One Hundred is the win
ner. The same committee ticket wins
in the Thirty-third, Thirty-fifth and
Thirty-ninth districts.
In the Fortieth District is a bitter
fight between two factions of the Com
mittee of One Hundred. Hassett leads
one faction and Rock 'the other. Re
turns-indicate the success of the Has
sett faction, although E. P. Troy and
W. H. Byington are, on the Rock ticket.
Democrats who are on the Inside as
sert that Phelan's Influence caused
many Republicans in this strong Re
publican district to get in the fight be
hind the Hassett faction.
The Committee of One Hundred elects
the delegates in the Forty-first District,
defeating Rainey and Buckley. The
committee also wins in the Forty-sec
ond and Forty-fifth districts.
While the bosses, Rainey and Lani
gan, may control a block of delegates
in the Democratic Convention the Com
mittee of One Hundred's following ex
ceeds the combined strength of the
bosses.
At the Buckley headquarters, after
the result- was announced, what few
members were present wore a look of
disappointment. Representatives of the
different districts agreed that they had
had an even fight and that the silent
vote had done the work.
Buckley, ln sympathizing with them,
said: " **-_/-.= *;;-
"Well, boys, we did the best we could
and we were beaten out by a popular
young man who is the choice of San
Francisco.
"This is not a victory for the Exam
iner-Rainey combination, nor did the
Fire Department knock us out. It was
simply this: Mayor Phelan told the
community that he wanted a certain
ticket elected and the people did his
bidding.
"He is a popular young man and he
has the confidence of the people, and
there is no doubt that he will place a
ticket in the field that will sweep
everything in sight on next election
day." * '
When one of Mr. Buckley's followers
spoke up and asked what the next
move would be, Mr. Buckley said:
"The only thing we can do. boys, is
to take off our coats on election day,
fall In line and vote the Democratic
ticket."

PHELAN SAYS A FRANK
APPEAL ALWAYS WINS
C-. !**-*. HE confusion in the mind
of the average citizen, who
honestly sought the right,
JJ, was the only danger which
threatened the highly fav
orable result. The Primary Law
Itself worked satisfactorily. A
frank appeal to the people, where
every one has an equal chance,
always meets with a response
which reflects the wisdom and
right judgment of the community.
The great parties will be purified
from within, and no longer will
there be a stigma attached to po
litical activity.
The Democratic County Commit
tee has won a signal victory
which imposes on the convention
great responsibility, to which I am
sure the delegates will rise.
JAMES D. PHELAN.

McNAB GLORIES IN
THE PRIMARY LAW
Â£â .. â primary election law is
an assured success. It is a
reform that has done more
__â¢ for the people at one stroke
than any legislation attempt
ed or accomplished in this genera
tion except the Australian ballot
law, and it is of equal importance
with that great measure.
The result accomplished by the
Democratic County Committee,
otherwise known as the Committee
of One Hundred, is a vindication of
its course of uncompromising and
aggressive honesty. The commit
tee took the position that it would
not recognize or compromise with
Rainey any more than with Buck
ley. They were both partners in
the same transactions. It kept its
hands clean, having nothing to do
with either of them.
Its nominees last year kept their
pledges to the people, and as far
as power lay in them gave good
government to San Francisco.
There is every reason to suppose
that the ticket which this free con
vention of reputable citizens will
nominate will be the best ever of
fered to the voters of the city. I
believe that the victory for de
cency achieved by the committee
means that the good government
for which so many have been con
tending under all sorts of dis
couragements for many years has
at last been accomplished for San
Francisco. GAVIN McNAB.
INCIDENTS OF THE DAY.
Braunhart Bitterly Fought in
the Twenty - Ninth by the
Rainey-Buckley Combine.
The Twenty-eighth District was a hot
bed of excitement, though no trouble of a
serious nature occurred. The vote
throughout the district was heavy and
rather steady. The rivalry between Buck
ley's forces and Sam Rainey's push was
keen, and at times it looked as if the two
elements would get sadly tangled up. The
Third Precinct, in front of 207 Second
street, was the liveliest polling place In
the district. Larry Conlon, Buckley's
chief lieutenant in the Twenty-eighth,
looked after the blind man's Interests in
this precinct, and Henry Healey and Jack
Greeley had the Rainey-Phelan end in
their hands. The double-cross was neatly
executed by Buck's outfit, and his dele
gates as a result were fully fifty votes to
the good. At 11 o'clock Monday night on
of Rainey's representatives entered Do
lan's lodging-house and made a deal with
Brick whereby he would turn over fifty
voters who lodged in the house for 50 cent
a vote. Shortly after midnight Larry
Conlon is said to have sat down with
Brick and raised the price to $1. At an
rate when the polls* opened in the morn
ing four hacks were driven up to the door
of Brick's lodging-house and his colonists
were given a free ride to the polls. Each
held a Buckley ticket, and it is safe to
predict that they voted it straight. John
nie Ferris, with the Fire Department be
hind him, had charge of the Rainey en
of it in the lower portion of the district.
Martin Kelly made his usual fight in the
First Precinct, at 313 Main street, and in
the upper end of the district Barney
Dougherty and Peg-leg Daly marshaled
the regular Republican forces. The op
posing Central Republican League faction
was largely in evidence all over the dis
trict, but voted silently.
When the polls closed it was though
that Sammy Braunhart's ticket in the
Twenty-ninth District was snowed under.
Rainey's adherents and the Buckley fac
tion combined and made a bitter battle
against Braunhart's ticket. "Anything to
beat" Braunhart," was the cry*.
About 4 o'clock in the afternoon a re
port was circulated about the district that
Sammy Braunhart was weeping bitter
tears at the corner of Fourth and Harri
son streets. .Matt Lally, his assistant-ln
chief, at once dispatched a messenger to
ascertain whether or not the report was
correct. The vote was quite heavy all
over the district and seemed to be evenly
divided between the Democrats and Re
publicans. Windy Dick Creighton, Tom
Brosnan and Jim Farrell made Buckley's
battle, and Jimmie Maher and Joe Plunk
ctt had charge of Braunhart's end. John
nie Glynn and Charlie Corey had charge
of the regulars, and they were given a
warm battle by the representatives of
the Central Republican organization. The
voting was done in an orderly manner de
spite the bitter rivalry existing between
the opposing factions.
A fight was narrowly averted in the
Sixteenth precinct of the Thirtieth Dis
trict early in the forenoon, the presence
of four husky policemen alone preventing
a serious outbreak. The row started over
the vote cast by John Bigelow, a Buckle
Democrat, who registered from Alex
Greggains' house. He was denounced by
J. H. Hogan, challenger for the regular
Democrats, and arrested. Greggains ap
peared at the polling place soon after ac
companied by a gang of strikers, evident
ly bent upon making trouble. Hot words
were exchanged and Hogan threatened
to brain Greggains with a chair. At this
juncture the police interfered and hus
tled the entire outfit out into the street.
In the melee one of the ward heelers re
ceived a blow on the nose that started the
blood flowing, but he could not tell who
hit him and no arrests were made. Kai
n.y had combined with the Regular Dem
ocrats, and his henchmen, under the lead
ership of Assistant Chief Dougherty of
the Fire Department, made a desperate
effort to defeat Greggains. The former
claimed a victory over the Buekleyites
early In the game. The latter, however,
put up a strong fight and Greggains
claimed he would carry the district for
the blind boss. This Is a strong Demo
cratic district and the Republicans polled
a comparatively small percentage of the
vote. The Citizens' Republican ticket had
Three - Cornered Fight
Leaves Democrats
in a Tangle.
Superior Leadership of Gavin
McNab Downs Smaller
Bosses.
UPRISING AGAINST
BOSSES, SAYS DODGE
WITH the natural feeling of
exhilaration which now
controls me it is hard for
me to collect my thoughts
sufficiently to tell how it happened.
But I will say that the new pri
mary law is one of the factors that
made the overturning of the bosses
possible. The new law is a most
unqualified success. If it were
not for the protection it gives
the voters in the way of secrecy
and by compelling all who attempt
to have a voice in the selection of
the delegates to establish their
identity and right to cast a vote,
the result of; to-day's election
might have been different. This
is demonstrated by the returns,
for in the districts that were hith
erto entirely under the control of
the bosses we have won out by the
largest majorities. ,
"I attribute our victory to the
fact that we as American citizens
believe in self-representation. The
same spirit that prompted our
forefathers to cast off the yoke of
Great Britain prompted us to-day
to cast off the yoke of bossism.
It has become revolting to the peo
ple to think that they had to go to
the polls and have pointed out to
them the men for whom they were
told they had to vote. That is what
caused this uprising.
"The Central Republican Com
mittee has no bossâ no one has
had more to say in its councils
than another. We have all worked
in harmony and for what we
thought was for the best interests
of the people, and with that spirit
we could not help but win.
"I am not surprised at the result.
The people were bound to be the
victors If they were only given a
chance and the new primary law
gave them that opportunity. It
forces upon us the conviction that
the motto on our great American
dollar should be not only 'In God
We Trust,' but 'In God and the
People We Trust.'
"No railroad influence will be
felt at our convention and no such
Influence has been shown in our
campaign. If they have helped us
it has been voluntary on their part,
without our knowledge and be
cause they thought our victory
would be best for the city. No in
fluence has been back of us but
that of God and the people. I
would have been ashamed of the
city and the people who live here
if the result had been otherwise.
"I cannot say who will be our
nominees; that matter rests entire
ly with the convention. I feel con
fident, however, that our delegates
will select the best list of nom
inee- ever presented to the voters
of San Francisco. Clean and able
men will be chosen and our ticket
will be elected from the Mayor
right down the line. Last year
Mayor Phelan won by the votes of
the" very Republicans who this
year vanquished the bosses by
starting this great reform move
ment. They will cast their votes
at the next election for our nom
inees and with their support I can
not figure how we can possibly
lose.
"Care will be exercised in the
selection of our county committee.
Tt should be composed of men who ,
are ready and willing to give their ,
time to the party; to guard it i
against entanglements; to protect <
the rights of the people, and give i
the voters the fullest opportunity '
to express their will. The commit- :
tee will be composed of repfesen- ,
tative men.
"ZENAS U. DODGE."

no opposition and but little attention was
paid to it. The Socialists also cut very
little figure in the district.
* Despite the fact that there were six
tickets in the field in the Thirty-fifth As
sembly District, not a protest or a harsh
word was heard during the hours of "vot
ing The representatives of the different
tickets in the various precincts were all
neighbors and acted in a neighborly man
ner toward each other. If a man was
seen approaching the polling place there
was no rush made to capture his vote
Instead he was greeted as a friend and as
such cast his fortunes with any ticket he
chose When the booths were opened at
6 o'clock the tide of voters began to flow
in Nearly half the ballots cast in the
precincts were voted between the hours of
8 and 10 o'clock. Fifty per cent of the
votes in the district were cast without
of bad whisky and a
sofdle? create,! considerable excitement at
the polling place of the E nty-lourtn

electlor booth, and it required the com
bined efforts of the policeman and sev
eral of the election officers to subdue him
and prepare him for the patrol wagon.
*In the Eighty-fourth. Eighty-nfib and
Eighty-sixth^.precincts there was but lit
tle outward interest displayed in the
contest, although the vote was unusually
lfl___ Most of the voters went to the
noils with the pasters in their pockets,
and after casting their ballots wandered
away as quietly and as quickly as pos-
Si The car men who voted at the polling
place at 406 Maple street were enthusi
astic upholders of the Buckley contin
gent. William Hoff Cook. Leo Parks,
fate of Oakland, and Franz Jacobv, the
insurance man. lined up on the same side.
They had practically no opposition.
WESTERN ADDITION.
The election in the Thirty-seventh As
-1 sembly District passed off quietly. With
the exception of a drunken rowdy, who
attempted to run things to suit himself
In the Fifty-fifth Precinct and who was
promptly suppressed, nothing of an ex
citing nature occurred.
â  The Republicans had two tickets in the
fieid and the Democrats three, namely,
the Buckley ticket, the Committee of One
Hundred and the Independent Democrats.
The supporters of the Buckley ticket
i worked indefatigably in the interest of
' the "blind boss.'' and at the close of the
polls they declared that the count would
: show that they had won the day.
Considerable enthusiasm was manifest-
I ed in the election in the Thirty-eighth
Assembly District. Some time before the
polls were opened motley crowds assem
bled in front of the different Dooths. ap
parently anxious to cast their ballots.
The Republicans had two tickets In the
fleld and the Democrats a similar num
: ber. The vote was unusually heavy, over
i 250 being polled before 10 o'clock. Several
political heelers attempted to dictate to
i the Judge of the election in the Sixty
j sixth Precinct, but were promptly driven
i off by the police.
